Third Objection: Body as a Universal
Taftazani refutes the third objection that body's universality entails actual infinite individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani refutes the claim that body as a universal implies actual infinite individuals.

Core Idea
Universality implies potential multiplicity, not actual infinite existence.

The third and most sophisticated objection argues that 'body' is a universal concept, so its possible instances are infinite — and due to divine emanation, they actually exist. Taftazani responds by distinguishing potential from actual infinity, showing that universality only allows for conceptual multiplicity, not real coexistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection carefully
Notice the opponent's argument: body is a universal like other universals, its instances are infinite, and they exist due to emanation.
This is a metaphysical argument linking universality to actuality.
2
Understand the refutation's first part
Taftazani says universality only implies the possibility of many individuals in the mind, not their actual existence.
He distinguishes between conceptual possibility and actual existence.
3
Understand the second part
He adds that the possibility of infinite individuals means they are not limited in potential, not that they all exist together.
This resolves the confusion between potential and actual infinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال احتج المخالف بوجوه: الأول: أن ما يلي الجنوب غير ما يلي الشمال وما يوازي ربع العالم أقل مما يوازي نصفه فلا يكون عدماً محضاً . قلنا مجرد وهم والثاني : أن الواقف على طرف العالم إما أن يمكنه مد اليد فثمة بعد، أو لا فثمة مانع . قلنا : لا يمكن لعدم الشرط لا لوجود المانع . الثالث: أن الجسم كلي لا ينحصر في شخص، فلا تتناهى أفراده الممكنة كسائر الكليات، بل توجد لعموم الفيض. قلنا الكلية لا تقتضي سوى إمكان كثرة أفراده المفروضة بالنظر إلى مفهومه، ولا ينافي امتناع كلها أو بعضها بموجب كلزوم المحالات المذكورة، على أن معنى إمكان افراده الغير المتناهية ليس اجتماعها في الوجود على ما هو المطلوب، بل عدم انتهائها إلى حد لا يمكن بعده وجود فرد آخر
He said: The opponent argued with [several] points: The first: That what is adjacent to the south is different from what is adjacent to the north, and what corresponds to a quarter of the world is less than what corresponds to half of it, so it is not pure non-existence. We said: [This is] mere illusion. The second: That the one standing at the edge of the world either can extend his hand, so there is an extension [beyond], or cannot, so there is an obstacle. We said: It cannot [be extended] due to the absence of the condition, not due to the presence of an obstacle. The third: That body is a universal [kulli] not restricted to a single individual, so its possible individuals are not finite, like other universals, but rather exist due to the generality of emanation [fayd]. We said: Universality implies only the possibility of a multiplicity of its individuals that are assumed with respect to its concept, and it does not contradict the impossibility of all or some of them due to the necessitation of the mentioned absurdities. Moreover, the meaning of the possibility of its infinite individuals is not their simultaneous existence in reality, as is demanded, but rather that they have no limit beyond which another individual cannot exist.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage presents three objections from someone who defends the possibility of infinite dimensions, along with their refutations. The first objection claims that since different directions (south/north) and different sizes (quarter/half) of the world imply differentiation, space cannot be pure nothingness. The refutation dismisses this as a mere illusion—differentiation does not prove actual extension. The second objection argues that if someone stands at the edge of the world, either they can extend their hand (meaning there is space beyond) or they cannot (then there is a barrier). The refutation states that the hand cannot extend not because of a barrier, but because the condition for extension (the existence of space) is absent. The third objection argues that 'body' is a universal concept, not restricted to a single individual; hence its possible instances are infinite, just like other universals, and they exist due to the generality of divine emanation. The refutation clarifies that universality implies only the abstract possibility of multiple instances, not their actual existence. The infinite individuals are only potential; their actual coexistence would lead to the absurdities already proven. In other words, possibility of infinite does not entail actual infinite existence.

Key Terms
الكلي (al-kulli)
الفيض (al-fayd)
العدم المحض (al-'adam al-mahd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a universal concept having infinite possible instances means those instances must exist in reality.
Correction: Universality only entails conceptual possibility; actual existence requires additional proof and may be impossible due to absurdities.

Checkpoint
What does Taftazani say is the correct meaning of 'possibility of infinite individuals' for a universal like body?
A) They all exist simultaneously in the world.
B) There is no limit to the number of individuals that could exist, but not all at once.
C) They exist in the mind only.
D) They are impossible.

Key Takeaway
A universal concept implies potential multiplicity, not actual infinite existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often assume that because something is possible, it will happen. But this lesson shows that possibility does not guarantee actuality. Where in your life have you confused potential with actual, and how will you now distinguish between what is merely possible and what is real?
?
When you think of God's generosity (fayd), do you expect it to produce every possible thing at once? This lesson corrects that expectation. How does limiting your expectation of God's acts to what is actually given change your gratitude or contentment?
?
The opponent in this lesson made a logical mistake that had serious consequences for understanding creation. What mistake are you currently making in your own beliefs about God's power and creation, and how will you use this lesson's method of precise thinking to correct it starting today?

Objection and Answer on Motion-Rest Necessity
Author refutes an objection against the necessity of motion or rest for bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the impossibility of a body being devoid of motion or rest, and how the author defends the necessity by clarifying the meaning of 'kown' and the nature of pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Motion and rest cannot both be absent; the objection misinterprets 'precedence' in pre-eternity.

This passage presents a sophisticated objection to the argument that every body must be either in motion or at rest. The objector claims that in pre-eternity, the notion of 'precedence' becomes problematic, making motion impossible. The author responds by carefully distinguishing between a 'kown' that is preceded and one that is not, and clarifying that pre-eternity is not a temporal state that can be 'before' something. This lesson unpacks that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the objection-and-answer structure.
2
Identify the objection's two claims
The objector argues that the first moment of rest requires a prior state, and that pre-eternity precludes such precedence. Find these two points.
The objection also denies that a kown not preceded in the same location is motion.
3
Understand the author's reply
The author restricts the discussion to kowns that are preceded by another kown. A kown with no prior kown is temporal, which proves the desired conclusion. Then he refutes the pre-eternity objection by defining al-azal as negation of a beginning.
The author's definition of pre-eternity is crucial.
4
Reflect on the concept of pre-eternity
Consider how the author's explanation that al-azal is not a temporal state but the absence of a beginning resolves the objection. Compare with al-abad (endlessness).
This clarifies that 'precedence' in pre-eternity is meaningless.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كذلك لما مرّ، وإنما قيد بالوجودي لأن عدم الحادث قديم يزول إلى الوجود إذ دليل امتناع عدم القديم . وهو أنه إما واجب أو مستند إليه بطريق الإيجاب، إنما قام في الموجود أما كون السكون وجودياً فلأنه من الأكوان، وأما كونه جائز الزوال فلأن كل جسم قابل للحركة . أما أولاً فلعدم نزاع الخصم في ذلك. وأما ثانياً فلأن الأجسام متماثلة فيجوز على كل منها ما يجوز على الآخر فإذا جاز الحركة على البعض بحكم المشاهدة جاز على الكل. وأما ثالثاً فلأن الأجسام إما بسائط وإما مركبات . فالبسائط يجوز على كل من أجزائها المتشابهة الحصول في الآخر، وما ذاك إلا بالحركة والمركبات يجوز على كل من بسائطها المقاسمة أن يكون تماسها الذي وقع بجزء من هذا يقع بسائر أجزائه المتشابهة حيز وذلك بالحركة . واعترض على ما ذكر في بيان امتناع خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون بأنه لو صح لزم أن يكون الجسم في أول السكون متحركاً أو ساكناً واللازم باطل قطعاً لاقتضاء كل منهما المسبوقية بكون آخر. وبأنا لا نسلّم أن الكون في حيز إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز كان حركة ، وإنما يلزم لو كان مسبوقاً بالكون في حيز آخر ، وهذا في الأزل محال ، لأن الأزلية تنافي المسبوقية بحسب الزمان . وأجيب بأن الكلام في الكون المسبوق بكون آخر للقطع بأن الكون الذي لا كون قبله حادث قطعاً وفيه المطلوب، وعلى هذا فالمنع ساقط لأن معنى الكلام أن الكون إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز بل في حيز آخر كان حركة ، وما ذكر من أن هذا ينافي الأزلية باطل، لأن الأزل كيس عبارة عن حالة زمانية لا حالة قبلها ليكون الكون فيه كوناً لا كون قبله ، بل معناه نفي أن يكون الشيء بحيث يكون له أول وحقيقته الاستمرار في الأزمنة المقررة الماضية، بحيث لا يكون له بداية، كما أن حقيقة الأبدية هو الاستمرار في الأزمنة الآتية لا إلى نهاية .
Likewise as passed. And it was restricted to the existential because the non-existence of the temporal is eternal, ceasing to existence, since the proof of the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal—and it is either necessary or dependent upon it by way of necessity—only stands in the existent. As for rest being existential, it is because it is among the 'acwān (modes). And as for it being possible of cessation, it is because every body is receptive of motion. As for the first, it is due to the lack of dispute of the opponent in that. As for the second, it is because bodies are similar, so whatever is possible for one of them is possible for the other; if motion is possible for some by virtue of observation, it is possible for all. As for the third, it is because bodies are either simple or composite. As for the simples, it is possible for each of their homogeneous parts to obtain in the other, and that is only by motion. As for the composites, it is possible for each of their simple components that their contact which occurred with a part of this one should occur with all its homogeneous parts in a location, and that is by motion. And an objection was raised against what was mentioned in explaining the impossibility of a body being devoid of motion and rest: that if it were valid, it would necessitate that the body at the first moment of rest be either moving or at rest, and the consequent is absolutely false, because each of them requires being preceded by another kown (existence). And (the objection is) that we do not grant that the kown in a location, if not preceded by a kown in that location, is motion; it would only be necessary if it were preceded by a kown in another location, and this in pre-eternity is impossible, because pre-eternity precludes being preceded in time. And it was answered: the discussion concerns the kown preceded by another kown, because it is certain that the kown that has no kown before it is absolutely temporal, and therein lies the desired conclusion. And on this basis, the denial is invalid, because the meaning of the statement is that the kown, if not preceded by a kown in that location but in another location, is motion. And what was mentioned that this contradicts pre-eternity is false, because pre-eternity [kays] is an expression for a temporal state, not a state before it such that the kown in it would be a kown with no kown before it. Rather, its meaning is the negation that a thing has a beginning, and its reality is continuity in the determined past times such that it has no beginning, just as the reality of eternity (al-abad) is continuity in future times without end.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer unit. The author first argues that rest is existential and that bodies are similar, so motion is possible for all. Then an objection is raised: if a body were never devoid of motion or rest, then at the first moment of rest it would have to be either moving or at rest, which is absurd because both require a prior state. The objector also denies that a 'kown' not preceded by one in the same location is motion; it would only be motion if preceded in another location, but in pre-eternity there is no prior location, so motion is impossible. The author answers by focusing on 'kown' preceded by another kown, since a kown with no prior is certainly temporal. Thus, the denial falls, because the objection's definition of motion requires a prior kown in a different location, which does not apply in pre-eternity. The author then clarifies that pre-eternity is not a temporal state with a 'before'; it is the negation of a beginning, like 'abad' (eternity to the future) is the negation of an end.

حركة
سكون
أزل
أبد
وجودي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre-eternity (al-azal) is a temporal moment that can be 'before' other things.
Correction: Pre-eternity is not a temporal state; it is the negation of a beginning. There is no 'before' in pre-eternity, so objections based on temporal precedence do not apply.

Checkpoint
Why does the author insist that the discussion of kown (occurrence) in the objection must be about a kown preceded by another kown?
A) Because a kown with no prior kown is temporal, proving the desired conclusion.
B) Because the objector only accepts preceded kowns.
C) Because motion requires two kowns in the same location.
D) Because pre-eternity cannot be described in temporal terms.

Key Takeaway
The key to resolving the objection is to realize that pre-eternity is not a temporal state, and that the term 'kown' in the argument refers to a kown preceded by another, which ensures the conclusion of motion or rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that pre-eternity (al-azal) is not a temporal state but the denial of a beginning. Do you ever imagine God's existence as stretching 'backward' on a timeline? How might this misconception affect your awe of Him? Today, commit to replacing any mental image of a 'before God' with the reality of His absolute beginninglessness.

The objection in this passage tried to use a false 'first moment' in pre-eternity to undermine the argument. What would it cost you to abandon that excuse and accept the contingency of your own existence before God?

The author says a kon with no prior kon is certainly temporal (hadith). Think of a deeply ingrained habit or belief you have never questioned—something you treat as if it has always been there. If you admitted it has a beginning, would that change how you evaluate it? What would it mean for your repentance if you traced every sin back to its first moment?

Refuting the Eternity of Motion's Essence
Author disproves the philosophers' claim that motion's essence is eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument against the philosophers' claim that the essence of motion is pre-eternal.

Core Idea
Motion's essence cannot be eternal because it requires being preceded, which eternity excludes.

The philosophers argue that while each motion is preceded, the essence of motion itself is eternal. Our author counters with a twofold proof that the essence of motion cannot be pre-eternal, drawing on the very definition of motion as change. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Understand the philosophers' stance: they deny the earlier proof applies to the essence of motion, claiming that every motion is preceded but the essence is eternal.
The objector also denies the major premise that whatever is not devoid of events is temporal.
2
Identify the first proof
The author argues that pre-eternity (al-azaliyya) precludes being preceded, but motion necessarily involves being preceded (change from state to state). Hence, motion cannot be eternal.
This proof targets the definition of motion itself.
3
Identify the second proof
If the universal essence of motion were eternal, then some particular instance would have to be eternal, but by consensus no particular motion is eternal.
This uses the principle that universals exist only in particulars.
4
Reflect on the significance
These proofs show that both the essence and particulars of motion are temporal, refuting the philosophers' view.
This supports the author's overall case for the createdness of the world.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل ما ذكرتم من دليل امتناع الأزلية إنما يقوم في كل جزئي من جزئيات الحركة ولا يدفع مذهب الحكماء وهو أن كل حركة مسبوقة بحركة أخرى لا إلى بداية والفلك متحرك أزلاً وأبداً بمعنى أنه لا يقرر زمان إلا وفيه شيء من جزئيات الحركة، وهذا معنى كون ماهية الحركة أزلية. وحينئذ يرد المنع على الكبرى أيضاً، أي لا نسلم أن ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث، وإنما يلزم لو كانت تلك الحوادث متناهية، فلا بد من بيان امتناع تعاقب الحوادث من غير بداية ونهاية على ما هو رأيهم في حركات الأفلاك وأوضاعها. أجيب: أولاً بإقامة البرهان على امتناع أن تكون ماهية الحركة أزلية وذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أن الأزلية تنافي المسبوقية ضرورة، والمسبوقية من لوازم ماهية الحركة وحقيقتها لكونها عبارة عن التغير من حال إلى حال، ومنافي اللازم مناف للملزوم ضرورة. وثانيهما: بأن ماهية الحركة لو كانت قديمة أي موجودة في الأزل لزم أن يكون شيء من جزئياتها أزلياً، إذ لا تحقق للكلي إلا في ضمن الجزئي لكن اللازم باطل بالاتفاق. وثانياً: بإقامة البرهان على امتناع تعاقب الحوادث الغير المتناهية وذلك أيضاً لوجهين: أحدهما: طريق التطبيق وهو أن نفرض جملة من الحوادث المتعاقبة من
For if it is said: what you have mentioned of the proof of the impossibility of pre-eternity only applies to each particular instance of motion, and does not refute the doctrine of the philosophers, which is that every motion is preceded by another motion without beginning, and the celestial sphere moves pre-eternally and post-eternally, meaning that no time is determined except that there is in it some particular motion, and this is the meaning of the essence of motion being pre-eternal. Then the denial applies also to the major premise, i.e., we do not grant that whatever is not devoid of events is itself temporal; it would only follow if those events were finite. So one must demonstrate the impossibility of the succession of events without beginning and end, according to their view regarding the motions of the celestial spheres and their configurations. It was answered: First, by establishing a proof of the impossibility of the essence of motion being pre-eternal, and that from two aspects: One: pre-eternality necessarily precludes being preceded, and being preceded is a necessary concomitant of the essence and reality of motion, because it is the change from one state to another, and that which precludes a necessary concomitant precludes the essence necessarily. Two: if the essence of motion were eternal, i.e., existent in pre-eternity, then some particular instance of it would have to be pre-eternal, since the universal has no actuality except within a particular, but the consequent is false by consensus. Second, by establishing a proof of the impossibility of the succession of infinite events, also from two aspects: One: the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq), which is that we assume a set of successive events from

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection-and-answer. The objector (philosophers) denies that the earlier proof against pre-eternity applies to the essence of motion itself, since each individual motion is preceded by another, but the essence of motion is eternal in the sense that there is no time without some motion. Thus, the objector also denies the major premise that whatever is not devoid of events is temporal, since those events might be infinite. The author answers with two separate proofs: (1) The impossibility of the essence of motion being pre-eternal, argued from two aspects: (a) pre-eternity precludes being preceded, but motion necessarily involves being preceded (change from state to state), so motion cannot be eternal; (b) if the universal (essence) were eternal, a particular would have to be eternal, which is impossible by consensus. (2) The impossibility of an infinite succession of temporal events, also from two aspects, beginning with the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq): assuming two infinite series starting from the present and going back, then matching them one-to-one, leading to a contradiction. The text cuts off before completing the second proof.

The Correspondence Argument Against Infinite Regress
Al-Taftazani explains the correspondence argument against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq) demonstrates that an infinite series of past events is logically impossible.

Core Idea
Comparing two infinite series leads to a contradiction, proving finitude.

Al-Taftazani now presents the first of two rigorous proofs against the philosophers' claim that the world could have an infinite past. This argument, known as the method of correspondence, is a clever thought experiment that exposes the absurdity of an actual infinite series of events. By mentally pairing two different infinite series, we uncover a logical contradiction that forces us to conclude that such a series cannot exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage introduces the correspondence method with a concrete example.
2
Understand the setup of the argument
Identify the two series: one starting from now and going back infinitely, another starting from the day of Noah's flood and also going back infinitely. Both are assumed to be infinite.
3
Follow the mental matching process
The argument mentally pairs each event in the first series with the corresponding event in the second. Consider what happens: either they match perfectly or they don't.
This step is crucial for the contradiction.
4
Analyze the two possibilities
If they match perfectly, then the whole (series from now) equals a part (series from flood), which is absurd. If they don't match, then one series must be finite, contradicting the assumption of infinity.
Both outcomes are impossible, so the premise of infinity is false.
5
Connect to the conclusion
The only way out is that both series are finite, proving that an infinite temporal regress cannot exist. This supports the necessity of a first event.
This conclusion is key for the overall proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانياً: بإقامة البرهان على امتناع تعاقب الحوادث الغير المتناهية وذلك أيضاً لوجهين: أحدهما: طريق التطبيق وهو أن نفرض جملة من الحوادث المتعاقبة من الآن، وأخرى من يوم الطوفان كل منهما لا إلى نهاية، ثم نطبق بينهما بحسب فرض العقل إجمالاً، بأن تقابل الأول من هذه بالأول من تلك وهكذا، فإما أن يتطابقا فيتساوى الكل والجزء أو لا فتنقطع الطوفانية ويلزم انتهاء الآنية لأنها لا تزيد عليها إلا بقدر متناه. وثانيهما: طريق التكافؤ، وهو أنا نفرض سلسلة من الحادث المعين الذي هو مسبوق بحادث وليس سابقاً على حادث آخر بمنزلة المعلول الأخير، فلضرورة تضايف السابقية والمسبوقية وتكافؤ المتضايفين في الوجود لزم أن تشتمل السلسلة على سابق غير مسبوق، وهو المنتهى، وتقرير آخر أنا نفرض سلسلة من المسبوقية وأخرى من السابقية ثم نطبق بينهما فتقع مسبوقية الأخير بإزاء سابقية ما فوقه، فيلزم الانتهاء إلى ما له السابقية دون المسبوقية تحقيقاً للتضايف.
And secondly, by establishing the proof of the impossibility of the succession of infinite events, and that also in two ways: One of them: the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq), which is that we posit one set of successive events from now, and another set from the day of the flood, each without end, and then we apply them to each other according to a general mental supposition, by matching the first of these with the first of those, and so on. Then either they correspond, and thus the whole and the part become equal, or they do not, and then the flood-series is cut off and the now-series necessarily ends because it does not exceed it except by a finite amount. The second: the method of equivalence (ṭarīq al-takāfu'), which is that we posit a chain consisting of a particular event that is preceded by an event and is not prior to another event, like the final effect. Then due to the necessity of the mutual relationship of priority and posteriority and the equivalence of correlates in existence, it necessarily follows that the chain includes a prior that is not preceded, and that is the terminus. Another formulation: we posit one chain of posteriority and another of priority, then we apply them to each other, so that the posteriority of the last corresponds to the priority of what is above it. This necessitates arriving at that which has priority without posteriority, realizing the correlation.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage contains the second part of the response to the philosophers' objection: the author proves the impossibility of an infinite succession of events (taʿāqub al-ḥawādith ghayr al-mutanāhiyya) through two arguments. The first argument is the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq). It posits two infinite series of events—one starting from the present moment and going back infinitely, and another starting from the day of Noah's flood and also going back infinitely. By mentally pairing each event in the first series with the corresponding event in the second series, we reach a contradiction: either the two series correspond perfectly, making the whole and part equal (which is impossible), or they do not correspond, which implies that the flood-series is finite and the present-series exceeds it by a finite amount, thus proving that both series must be finite. The second argument is the method of equivalence (ṭarīq al-takāfu'), which focuses on a specific event that is preceded by another event and is not prior to any other (like the last effect in a causal chain). Due to the necessary correlation (taḍāyuf) between priority and posteriority, the chain must contain a first member that is prior without being posterior—thus terminating the series. Alternatively, by positing two chains—one of posteriority and one of priority—and applying them to each other, we again reach a termination point. Both arguments demonstrate that an infinite regress is impossible.

Key Terms
تعاقب الحوادث
طريق التطبيق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the two infinite series could be different in size.
Correction: The argument shows that comparing two infinite sets leads to a contradiction, proving that actual infinites cannot exist in reality.

Checkpoint
What is the key contradiction in the correspondence argument?
A) The whole equals a part
B) The flood series is longer than the now series
C) Both series are finite
D) The now series has no end

Key Takeaway
The correspondence argument shows that an infinite series of past events leads to a logical contradiction, thus proving that the past must be finite.

?
The correspondence argument shows that an infinite regress is logically impossible. What in your life are you treating as if it had no beginning—like a cycle of behavior or a fear? How can you use this truth to break that cycle and start anew today?

The Equivalence Argument Against Infinite Regress
Al-Taftazani explains the equivalence argument against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the method of equivalence (ṭarīq al-takāfu') uses the relation of priority and posteriority to prove that a causal chain must have a first cause.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are correlates that demand a first term.

Al-Taftazani now presents the second argument against infinite regress, known as the method of equivalence. This argument focuses on the relationship between priority and posteriority in a causal chain. By analyzing the necessary correlation (taḍāyuf) between a 'prior' and a 'posterior', he demonstrates that any series of events must have a starting point—a cause that is itself uncaused. This argument is more abstract but equally powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage introduces the equivalence method and presents two formulations.
2
Understand the concept of correlation (taḍāyuf)
Priority and posteriority are relational terms that exist only together. If there is a posterior, there must be a prior, and vice versa.
This is a key logical principle.
3
Analyze the first formulation
Posit a chain of events ending with a final effect. Each event is preceded by another. Because of correlation, the chain must include a first event that is prior but not preceded.
4
Analyze the second formulation
Posit two chains: one of posteriority and one of priority. By matching them, we see that the last posterior corresponds to a priority above, forcing a term with priority only.
This reinforces the conclusion.
5
Connect to the overall proof
Both formulations show that an infinite regress is impossible, leading to a first uncaused cause, which is a necessary step in proving God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And secondly, by establishing the proof of the impossibility of the succession of infinite events, and that also in two ways: One of them: the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq), which is that we posit one set of successive events from now, and another set from the day of the flood, each without end, and then we apply them to each other according to a general mental supposition, by matching the first of these with the first of those, and so on. Then either they correspond, and thus the whole and the part become equal, or they do not, and then the flood-series is cut off and the now-series necessarily ends because it does not exceed it except by a finite amount. The second: the method of equivalence (ṭarīq al-takāfu'), which is that we posit a chain consisting of a particular event that is preceded by an event and is not prior to another event, like the final effect. Then due to the necessity of the mutual relationship of priority and posteriority and the equivalence of correlates in existence, it necessarily follows that the chain includes a prior that is not preceded, and that is the terminus. Another formulation: we posit one chain of posteriority and another of priority, then we apply them to each other, so that the posteriority of the last corresponds to the priority of what is above it. This necessitates arriving at that which has priority without posteriority, realizing the correlation.

Commentary
This passage contains the second part of the response to the philosophers' objection: the author proves the impossibility of an infinite succession of events (taʿāqub al-ḥawādith ghayr al-mutanāhiyya) through two arguments. The first argument is the method of correspondence (ṭarīq al-taṭbīq). It posits two infinite series of events—one starting from the present moment and going back infinitely, and another starting from the day of Noah's flood and also going back infinitely. Additional Arguments Against the Eternity of Motion
Al-Taftazani enumerates additional proofs against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand six concise rational arguments that aim to prove the impossibility of an infinite past and the necessity of a beginning for motion and events.

Core Idea
Multiple logical arguments converge to show that an infinite series of events is impossible.

Al-Taftazani continues his refutation of the eternity of motion by presenting a series of concise arguments. Each argument targets a different aspect of the problem—composition, finitude, exhaustion, causality, and temporal ordering—to demonstrate that an infinite regress of events leads to contradictions. As you read, try to identify the logical structure of each argument and see how they collectively support the necessity of a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's concise style come through before diving into analysis.
The passage is incomplete at the end, but the arguments listed are still clear.
2
Identify each distinct argument
The passage enumerates several arguments. Count them and note the key phrase that introduces each one. There are at least five complete arguments.
Look for markers like 'وأن' which introduce new points.
3
Analyze the logical structure
For each argument, ask: what is the premise, and what conclusion does it lead to? Consider why each premise is meant to be self-evident or accepted.
Some arguments rely on concepts like 'قبول الزيادة والنقصان' (susceptibility to increase and decrease).
4
Connect to the theological context
Reflect on how these arguments support the kalām claim that the world is created and requires a free agent (al-mukhtār). The fourth argument explicitly links motion to contingency.
This reinforces the earlier discussion on the necessity of a temporal origin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة امتناع تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية ونهاية قال (وقد يذكر ) وجوه أخر مثل: أن حدوث كل يستلزم حدوث الكل، وأن قبول الزيادة والنقصان يستلزم التناهي، وأن عدم تناهي الحركات الماضية يستلزم امتناع انقضائها، وأن كل جزء من الحركة لزواله يجب أن يكون أثر المختار دون الموجب فيكون حادثة، وأن الحركة إن كانت مسبوقة بأخرى امتنع أزليتها، وإلا تحقق أوليتها، وأن كلا من جزئيات الحركة مسبوق بعدم أزلي فيتقارن عدماها في
Proofs of the impossibility of the succession of events without beginning and end. He said: And there are mentioned other aspects such as: that the occurrence of each entails the occurrence of the whole; and that susceptibility to increase and decrease necessitates finitude; and that the non-finiteness of past motions necessitates the impossibility of their being exhausted; and that every part of motion, due to its passing away, must be the effect of a free agent (al-mukhtār) rather than a necessitating cause (al-mūjib), thus being a contingent event; and that if motion is preceded by another, its eternity is impossible, otherwise its primacy would be realized; and that each of the particulars of motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence, so their two non-existences coincide in
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage lists additional concise arguments (wujūh ukhar) supporting the impossibility of an infinite succession of events without beginning or end. Each argument is stated briefly:
1. If each individual event occurs, the entire infinite series must also occur, but that leads to a contradiction because the whole would be composed of finite parts? Actually, the text says 'that the occurrence of each entails the occurrence of the whole' — implying that if each member of an infinite set is realized, the whole set is realized, which would be an actual infinite, considered impossible.
2. Anything that admits increase and decrease must be finite, so the series of past events, which can be increased by adding more events, must be finite.
3. If past motions are infinite, they can never be exhausted, yet we have reached the present, so they must be finite.
4. Each part of motion perishes, so it must be produced by a free agent (al-mukhtār) who chooses to create it at a specific time, not by a necessitating cause (al-mūjib) that would necessitate eternal emanation; thus each motion is a contingent event (ḥāditha).
5. If a particular motion is preceded by another motion, then that motion cannot be eternal; otherwise, if not preceded, it would be a first motion (a beginning), contradicting eternity.
6. Each particular of motion is preceded by an eternal non-existence (i.e., it did not exist from eternity), so the non-existences of all motions coincide in eternity, implying that the series cannot be infinite. The text cuts off at this point.

Misconception: One might think that an infinite past is logically harmless and does not lead to contradictions.
Correction: These arguments show that an actual infinite series of events leads to logical and metaphysical problems, such as the impossibility of traversing an infinite past, self-contradictions in composition, and the need for a free creator.

Second Proof: Infinite Regress of Movements
Dilemma proof and immediate objection regarding movement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof from temporal phenomena (the impossibility of an infinite regress of movements) and the objection that the proof only shows each movement is temporal, not that there is a first temporally originated thing.

Core Idea
The proof forces a beginning; the objection argues it only shows each movement is temporal.

Sanusi presents the second proof for the temporal origin of the world: a dilemma about any assumed movement. Either it is preceded by another movement or not; either way, movement has a beginning. An immediate objection challenges whether this proof actually establishes a first temporally originated thing. Let's examine the reasoning and the nuance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the proof and objection.
Notice the structure: two options followed by an objection starting with 'وورد'.
2
Understand the dilemma
The proof presents two possibilities for any assumed movement: either it is preceded by another movement (hence not eternal) or it is not preceded (hence it is the first movement, a beginning). In both cases, movement has a beginning.
The phrase 'فلا تكون أزلية' indicates the conclusion for the first horn.
3
See the conclusion
The proof concludes that movement has a beginning 'al-matlub' — the desired result. This establishes temporality of movement.
4
Consider the objection
The objection chooses the first horn (every movement preceded) but argues it only shows each particular movement is temporal, not that there is a first movement. The real dispute is whether the series reaches a first temporally originated thing.
Focus on the difference between proving temporality of each part and proving the existence of a first part.
5
Reflect on the real dispute
The objection highlights that the proof does not necessarily force an end to the regress. The discussion is about whether the series must have a first member.
This nuance prepares for further arguments in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها أن كل حركة تفرض لا تخلو من أن تكون مسبوقة بحركة أخرى، فلا تكون أزلية ضرورة سبق العدم عليها ، أو لا تكون مسبوقة بأخرى بل يتحقق حركة لا حركة قبلها، فتكون للحركة بداية وهو المطلوب. وورد بأنا نختار الأول ولا يفيد إلا حدوث كل من جزئيات الحركة، ولا نزاع فيه، وإنما النزاع في أن ينتهي إلى حادث لا يكون قبله حادث آخر.
And among them is that every movement that is assumed is not free from either being preceded by another movement, so it is not eternal necessarily due to the precedence of non-existence over it, or it is not preceded by another but rather a movement occurs with no movement before it, so movement has a beginning, and that is what is sought. And it was objected: we choose the first [possibility] and it only entails the temporal origination of each particular movement, and there is no dispute about that; rather the dispute is about whether it ends at a temporally originated thing before which there is no other temporally originated thing.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof and an immediate objection. The proof is a dilemma: any assumed movement is either preceded by another movement or not. If it is preceded, then it is not eternal because non-existence precedes it (since the preceding movement itself is temporal). If it is not preceded, then it is the first movement, giving movement a beginning. In either case, movement has a beginning, which is the desired conclusion. The objection states that we choose the first horn (that every movement is preceded by another), but that only proves that each particular movement is temporally originated, which is not disputed. The real dispute is whether there is an ultimate first movement that is not preceded by any other. The objection thus challenges whether the dilemma necessarily leads to a first movement.

Key Terms
حركة (movement)
مسبوقة (preceded)
أزلية (eternal)
بداية (beginning)
حادث (temporally originated)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the possibility of infinite regress avoids a beginning for movement, but the objection clarifies that the dispute is about reaching a first event, not about each event being temporal.
Correction: The objection shows that the proof's first horn only establishes that each movement is temporal if taken individually, but it does not necessarily require a first movement. The real issue is whether the series must terminate in an un-preceded temporally originated thing.

Checkpoint

C) The proof assumes movement is accidental rather than substantial.
D) The objection accepts the proof but calls it incomplete.

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes a beginning for movement, but the objection requires clarifying that the real dispute is about whether there is a first temporally originated thing, not merely the temporality of each movement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Third Proof: Succession of Events and Eternal Non-Existence
Reductio argument against eternal succession of events.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the third kalam proof that the universe must have a beginning, based on the impossibility of an infinite succession of events.

Core Idea
An eternal chain of events leads to impossible coexistence of opposites.

This passage presents the third proof against an eternal universe. It is a reductio ad absurdum: assuming an endless succession of events without a first event leads to a logical contradiction. The argument forces us to accept that events must have a beginning. Let us walk through the steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing to analyse. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the tight logical structure.
2
Identify the initial assumption
The argument assumes a succession of temporal events with no beginning. What follows from that assumption?
3
Follow the dilemma
The argument branches into two cases: either some existence occurs in eternity or none does. Work through each branch separately.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إذ لو تأخر شيء منها...' which forces co-eternity of non-existences.
4
Understand the conclusion
Both branches lead to either impossibility or the desired conclusion. This proves the original assumption false.
The phrase 'وهو المطلوب' signals the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها أنه لو فرضنا تعاقب الحوادث من غير بداية لكان كل منها مسبوقاً بعدم أزلي لأن ذلك معنى الحدوث، ويلزم اجتماع تلك العدمات في الأزل، إذ لو تأخر شيء منها عن الأزل لما كان أزلياً، وإذا اجتمعت العدمات في الأزل، فإن حصل شيء من الوجودات في الأزل لزم مقارنة السابق والمسبوق بل اجتماع النقيضين وهـو محال، وإن لم يحصل فهو المطلوب.
And among them is that if we suppose the succession of temporal events without a beginning, then each of them would be preceded by an eternal non-existence because that is the meaning of temporal origination, and it would necessitate the coming together of those non-existences in eternity, for if something of them were delayed from eternity, it would not be eternal. And when the non-existences come together in eternity, then if some existence occurs in eternity, it would necessitate the co-existence of the preceding and the preceded, rather the coming together of two opposites, and that is impossible; and if it does not occur, then that is what is sought.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This is the third proof, a reductio ad absurdum. It supposes an eternal succession of events without a first event. Each event, being temporally originated, is preceded by non-existence. Since there is no first event, each event's non-existence is eternal (i.e., it extends backward infinitely). This requires that all these non-existences coexist in eternity (since none is delayed from eternity). Then a dilemma follows: if any existence occurs in eternity, it would coincide with the eternal non-existences, leading to the co-existence of opposites (existence and non-existence) which is impossible. If no existence occurs in eternity, then the universe is eternally devoid of events, which is also the conclusion that the universe has a beginning (since no events in eternity means events began later). Thus, the original assumption of an eternal succession without beginning is false.

Key Terms
تعاقب الحوادث (succession of events)
عدم أزلي (eternal non-existence)
الوجودات (existences)
اجتماع النقيضين (coexistence of opposites)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The non-existences of different events are separate and cannot 'come together' in a single moment because they are not physical objects.
Correction: The argument does not require physical coexistence; it refers to logical coexistence in eternity. Since each non-existence is eternal, they all belong to the same eternal time frame. The contradiction arises if existence is introduced into this same time frame.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion if no existence occurs in eternity?
A) The assumption of eternal succession is confirmed.
B) The universe must have a beginning.
C) Opposites coexist.
D) The argument fails.

Key Takeaway
This proof shows that an eternal chain of events leads to a logical contradiction, so the universe must have had a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that an endless chain of events is impossible. Look at your own life—your habits, your decisions, your spiritual state. Are you living as if your actions have no ultimate cause, as if they just keep going without a beginning? What does it mean to you that your very existence requires a First Cause beyond yourself? Will you take a moment today to acknowledge that dependence and thank the One who began it all?
?
The argument forces a choice between a logical contradiction and accepting a beginning. Are there contradictions in your life—things you hold together that cannot logically coexist, like claiming faith in God while living as if He doesn't matter? What is one contradiction you will resolve today by submitting to the truth that the universe (and your life) has a purpose and a beginning from God?
?
The proof hinges on the impossibility of two opposites coexisting. When you say you believe in God but your actions show otherwise, you are trying to hold two opposites. How can you bring your life into alignment with the truth that the Creator is the only necessary existence? What is the first step you will take to let go of the falsehood and embrace the reality that everything depends on Him?

The Third Proof: Body as Effect of the Free Agent
The author states the third proof and its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof for the temporality of the body, which argues that the body is an effect of a free agent and therefore must be temporally originated.

Core Idea
The body's need for a creator proves it is an effect of the free agent.

This passage presents the third of several proofs for the temporality of the body. The author argues that since the body is an effect of a free agent, it must be temporally originated. He also clarifies that this argument relies on the principle that mere possibility is the cause of the need for an efficient cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the author builds the proof step by step.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage begins with the claim: the body is an effect of the free agent. Find this statement and note what it means.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author explains that every possible being needs an efficient cause, and the chain ends with the Necessary Being, who acts by choice. Notice that the effect of a free agent is temporal because it is preceded by intention.
This reasoning connects causality with temporality.
4
Note the condition
The author adds a condition: this proof is only valid for those who hold that mere possibility is the cause of the need for an efficient cause. Think about why this condition matters.
This highlights a key difference in theological approaches.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثالث) الجسم أثر الفاعل المختار أن الجسم أثر الفاعل المختار ابتداء أو انتهاء، لما سيجيء من إثبات قدرة الواجب فيكون حادثاً لما مر. لاخفاء في أن الجسم ، بل كل ممكن يحتاج إلى مؤثر، ولا بد من الانتهاء (۱) إلى الواجب تعالى . وسيجيء أنه فاعل الاختيار (۳) ، وقد سبق أن كل ما هو أثر المختار فهو حادث مسبوق بالقصد إلى إيجاده ولا يكون ذلك إلا حال عدمه وبهذا نثبت حدوث ما سوى الصانع (۳) من الجواهر والأعراض، وليشكل بصفاته القديمة. ولا يتم إلا على من يجعل سبب الاحتياج إلى المؤثر مجرد () الإمكان. وكذا الرابع إلا أنه لا يتوقف على إثبات كون الصانع مختاراً ، لكن يبتنى على المغلطة المشهورة. وهي أن تأثير المؤثر في الشيء حال وجوده تحصيل للحاصل، وقد عرفت(ه) حلها . وأما الخامس. فهو بعينه الأول إلا أنه بين فيه عدم خلو الجسم عن الحادث، بأنه لا يخلو عن مقدار مخصوص أو حيز مخصوص، وكل منهما حادث، لكونه أثر المختار. إذ نسبة الموجب إلى جميع المقادير والأحياز على السواء. ويردّ عليه : أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك باعتبار المادة أو الصورة أو عدد الجواهر (1) الفردة أو غير ذلك من الأسباب الخارجة .
He said (the third): The body is an effect of the free agent, that the body is an effect of the free agent, either initially or finally, because what will come of establishing the power of the Necessary Being, so it is temporal as previously mentioned. There is no concealment that the body, rather every possible, needs an efficient cause, and it must end (1) at the Necessary Being, exalted. And it will come that He is the agent by choice (3), and it has preceded that everything that is an effect of the free agent is temporal, preceded by intention to bring it into existence, and that is only in the state of its non-existence, and by this we prove the temporality of what is other than the Maker (3) of substances and accidents, and let it not be confused with His eternal attributes. And it is not complete except for one who makes the cause of need for the efficient cause mere possibility. And similarly the fourth, except that it does not depend on proving that the Maker is free, but it is based on the famous fallacy, which is that the influence of the efficient cause on a thing in the state of its existence is a realization of what is already realized, and you have known its solution. As for the fifth, it is the same as the first, except that he explains in it the non-emptiness of the body from the temporal, that it is not empty of a particular quantity or a particular location, and each of them is temporal, because it is an effect of the free agent, since the relationship of the necessitator to all quantities and locations is equal. And it is objected to him: that it is permissible that that is due to matter, or form, or the number of individual substances (1), or other external causes.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the temporality of the body and then introduces an objection. The author first states the claim: the body is an effect of the free agent, either initially (at the moment of creation) or finally (in its existence). This depends on the later proof of the power of the Necessary Being. He notes that every possible thing needs an efficient cause, and the chain ends with the Necessary Being. The author then reminds that it will be shown that the Necessary Being acts by choice, and that the effect of a free agent is temporal, preceded by intention, which only occurs when the thing is non-existent. Thus, everything other than the Maker (substances and accidents) is temporal. He cautions not to confuse this with God's eternal attributes. He notes that this argument only works for those who hold that the need for an efficient cause is due to mere possibility. He compares with the fourth and fifth proofs briefly: the fourth does not rely on proving the Maker is free but on a fallacy; the fifth is the same as the first but focuses on the body's non-emptiness from temporal accidents (quantity and location), which are temporal because they are effects of the free agent. Finally, an objection is raised: the temporality of quantity and location might be due to matter, form, individual substances, or other external causes, not necessarily the free agent's choice.

الفاعل المختار
الحادث
المؤثر
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The temporality of the body is proven by its composition or accidents.
Correction: The third proof argues that the body is temporal because it is an effect of a free agent, not because of its composition or accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, what is the basis for concluding that the body is temporally originated?
A) The body is composed of atoms that are temporal.
B) The body is an effect of a free agent, and every effect of a free agent is preceded by intention.
C) The body cannot be pre-eternal because that would lead to infinite regress.
D) The body is constantly changing.

Key Takeaway
The third proof establishes that the body must be temporally originated because it is an effect of a free agent, and everything that is an effect of a free agent is preceded by intention and thus temporal.

First Argument for the Pre-eternity of the World
The author presents the first argument used by those who hold the pre-eternity of the world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the first argument for the pre-eternity of the world based on the principle of the complete cause and the impossibility of delay.

Core Idea
If all necessary conditions are eternal, the world must be eternal; otherwise, infinite regress.

Theological debates about the world's pre-eternity often hinge on the relationship between cause and effect. This lesson examines the first argument advanced by those who claim the world is pre-eternal, which relies on the concept of the 'complete cause' (al-illah al-tammah). By understanding this argument, you will see how scholars of kalam frame the issue of temporal origination versus pre-eternity.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument in Arabic and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the precise wording.
Notice the logical structure: if... then... else...
2
Identify the two branches
The argument presents two possibilities: either all necessary conditions existed in pre-eternity or not.
Each branch leads to a conclusion.
3
Understand the principle of the complete cause
The key principle is that the effect cannot be delayed from a complete cause. Reflect on why this is considered impossible.
4
Consider the alternative: infinite regress
If a condition is temporal, the argument says we must examine that condition, leading to an infinite chain. Why is that problematic?
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
The refutation is not given here, but consider possible responses. Does the argument assume that all conditions are either eternal or temporal?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة القائلين بقدم العالم والرد عليها قال (تمسك القائلون) بقدم العالم بوجوه : الأول : أن جميع ما لا بد منه في وجود العالم إن كان حاصلاً في الأزل لزم وجوده، لامتناع التخلف عن تمام العلة التامة (۱) ، وإلا ننقل الكلام إلى ذلك الحادث فيتسلسل .
Proofs of those who hold the pre-eternity of the world and the refutation against them. He said: Those who hold the pre-eternity of the world adhere to aspects: The first: That all that is necessary for the existence of the world, if it was obtained in pre-eternity, its existence would be necessary, because of the impossibility of delay from the complete cause (1), and if not, we would transfer the discourse to that temporal [condition] and there would be a regress.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument advanced by proponents of the pre-eternity of the world. The argument is: If all the necessary conditions for the existence of the world were present in pre-eternity (الأزل), then the world would necessarily have to exist at that time, because it is impossible for the effect to be delayed from a complete cause (العلة التامة). If those conditions were not all present in pre-eternity, then some condition is temporal, and we would have to investigate that temporal condition, leading to an infinite regress. This argument attempts to force a choice: either the world is pre-eternal (if all conditions were eternal) or there is an infinite regress of temporal conditions, which is considered impossible. The refutation of this argument is not included in this passage.

Key Terms
قدم العالم
الأزل
العلة التامة
التسلسل
التخلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might assume the argument is decisive, concluding that the world must be pre-eternal.
Correction: The argument appears strong, but theologians later refute it by denying that the cause was complete in pre-eternity or by other means not shown here. The argument is not accepted as conclusive by the author.

Checkpoint
The first argument for the pre-eternity of the world is based on which principle?
A) The impossibility of infinite regress.
B) The impossibility of delay in the effect of a complete cause.
C) The impossibility of temporal origination.
D) The necessity of a creator.

Key Takeaway
The first argument for the pre-eternity of the world forces a choice between the world being eternal given eternal conditions or an infinite regress of temporal conditions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
This passage presents an argument that is being refuted. Are you able to separate the presentation of a false argument from your own acceptance of it? How often do you passively accept ideas that sound logical without testing them against what you know to be true from Allah?

Divine Will as the Foundation of Creation
Answers the objection that the world's creation depends on successive events by affirming divine will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection of dependence on successive events is refuted by affirming the role of divine will (al-irada) as the determining factor in creation.

Core Idea
Divine will breaks the chain of infinite causes.

This passage addresses a key objection against the temporal origination of the world: if every new event requires a prior cause, wouldn't the world need an infinite chain of events? The author, al-Sanusi, responds by clarifying that the world's creation does not depend on a material chain but on an act of divine will that can preponderate at any moment. This lesson unpacks that response and shows how it preserves both the necessity of the world's dependence on God and His free choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author first presents a common objection before giving his answer.
The objection is implied: that the world's creation would require an infinite series of events.
2
Identify the weak premise
The author points out that the claim that every temporal event requires matter (madda) is weak. Identify why this is crucial to the argument.
By rejecting the necessity of matter, the ground is cleared for the role of will.
3
Understand the role of divine will
The author asserts that the indispensable condition is divine will, which can give preponderance at any time without another determinant. Consider how this solves the problem of infinite regress.
Will is self-sufficient; it does not need another cause to choose.
4
Reflect on the compatibility with free choice
The passage concludes that the necessity arising from God's will does not negate His free choice but confirms it. Think about why this is an important theological point.
Necessity here is not forced but flows from God's own attribute of will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب : النقص بالحادث اليومي، وليس الفرق بأنه يستند الى حوادث فلكية متعاقبة لا إلى نهاية دفعاً له على أن الكلام في العالم الجسماني، فلم لا يجوز أن يكون حدوثه مشروطاً بتطورات أو إرادات. وبالجملة : حوادث متعاقبة لأمر مجرد. وقد سبق أن حديث لزوم المادة لكل حادث ضعيف، والمنع بأنه لم لا يجوز أن يكون من جملة مالا بد منه الإرادة التي من شأنها الترجيح أي وقت شاء من غير افتقار الى مرجح آخر، ويكون تعلق الإرادة أيضاً بمجرد الإرادة، ووجوب العالم بهذا التعليق لا ينافي اختيار الصانع بل يحققه
The answer: The deficiency is with the daily temporal event, and the distinction is not that it is based on successive astronomical events without end, as a rebuttal to it, because the discussion concerns the physical world. So why is it not permissible that its temporal origination be conditioned by developments or wills? In summary: successive events for a sheer purpose. It has already preceded that the claim of the necessity of matter for every temporal event is weak, and the prevention is that why is it not permissible that among what is indispensable is the will, which is characterized by giving preponderance at any time the agent wills, without being in need of another preponderant, and the attachment of the will is also by mere will, and the necessity of the world through this conditioning does not contradict the free choice of the Maker but rather confirms it.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents an answer to an objection: The argument that the world's temporality is similar to daily events (al-hadith al-yawmi) is countered. The key point is that the world's creation could depend on a series of successive events or divine wills, not on a single material cause. The author emphasizes that the claim that every temporal event requires matter is weak (da'if). Instead, the indispensable condition is divine will (al-irada), which can give preponderance at any time without needing another determinant. The attachment of the will is also by mere will, so the necessity arising from this conditioning does not negate God's free choice (ikhtiyar) but rather confirms it. This refutes the objection that the world would require an infinite regress of causes.

الاختيار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that temporal events necessarily require matter and an infinite chain of causes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that divine will can break the chain: the world's creation is conditioned by will, which gives preponderance without needing another cause, thus avoiding infinite regress.

Checkpoint

A) Matter and astronomical movements
B) Divine will (al-irada)
C) A chain of successive events without end

Key Takeaway
Divine will is the indispensable condition that allows the world to exist without an infinite chain of causes, thereby preserving God's free choice.

The Scope of the Proof Against Infinite Regress
Sanusi limits the proof against infinite regress to ordered principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between ordered principles and transient instruments, and why the proof against infinite regress applies only to the former.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible for ordered causes, not for transient means.

This passage clarifies a crucial limitation in the argument against infinite regress. While astronomical configurations and planetary conjunctions may form an endless chain of renewal, not every sequence falls under the same rational impossibility. Sanusi distinguishes between ordered principles (mabadi' mutarattiba) whose existence requires a common order, and transient instruments (mu'addat mutasarrima) that pass away individually.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the full Arabic text carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the key terms and how the argument unfolds.
The passage ends with 'as previously mentioned,' indicating a connected discussion.
2
Identify the two types of sequences
Look for the contrast between 'ordered principles' (mabadi' mutarattiba) and 'transient instruments' (mu'addat mutasarrima). Understand the difference: ordered principles depend on each other in a chain, while transient instruments are independent in existence.
The author also mentions that some proofs cover both types, but the initial proof only applies to ordered ones.
3
Understand why the limitation matters
Reflect on why the proof against infinite regress cannot be applied blindly to all sequences. This prevents a common misunderstanding in kalam arguments.
This distinction preserves the logical soundness of arguments for the existence of a First Cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأوضاع الفلكية والاتصالات الكوكبية، ووجود كل منها مشروط بانقضاء الآخر لا إلى بداية على سبيل التجدد والانقضاء دون الترتيب في الوجود، على ما هو شأن العلل والمعلولات ليلزم التسلسل المحال . فإن البرهان إنما قام على استحالة التسلسل في المبادىء المترتبة دون المعدات المتصرمة لأنا نقول بعض البراهين كالتطبيق والتضائف يتناول ما يضبطها الوجود مترتبة سواء كانت مجتمعة أو متصرمة كما آنفاً . سبق
And the astronomical configurations and planetary conjunctions, the existence of each is conditioned by the passing away of the other without beginning, in the manner of renewal and cessation without order in existence, as is the case with causes and effects, thereby necessitating the impossible infinite regress. For the proof (al-burhan) has only been established on the impossibility of infinite regress in ordered principles, not in transient instruments, because we say some proofs such as [al-tatbeeq] and [al-tadaaf] encompass whatever existence comprehends as ordered, whether they are simultaneous or successive, as previously mentioned. Preceded.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage discusses the impossibility of infinite regress in causal chains. The author states that astronomical configurations and planetary conjunctions each require the prior ending of the other, without a beginning, in a cycle of renewal and cessation, similar to causes and effects. This would entail an impossible infinite regress. However, the proof against infinite regress applies only to ordered principles (mabadi' mutarattiba), not to transient instruments (mu'addat mutasarrima). Some proofs like tatbeeq (application) and tadaaf (correlativity) encompass all existents whether simultaneous or successive. The author then says 'as previously mentioned' and 'preceded'.

Key Terms
برهان (burhan)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
مبادئ مترتبة (mabadi' mutarattiba)
معدات متصرمة (mu'addat mutasarrima)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof against infinite regress applies to any kind of sequence, including cycles of transient events.
Correction: The proof only applies to ordered principles where each step is a true cause for the next in a hierarchical chain, not to transient instruments that pass away independently.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the proof against infinite regress applies to which type of entities?
A) Transient instruments only
B) Ordered principles only
C) Both ordered principles and transient instruments
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The proof against infinite regress is not universal; it is limited to ordered causal chains, not to all sequences of events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, you experience endless cycles of habits and actions, some seem ordered (cause and effect) and others mere repetitions. Are you treating all sequences as if they require a 'first cause' from you, when perhaps some are just transient instruments that need not be traced to a beginning? How does this lesson challenge your approach to personal change?
?
The author distinguishes between what the proof covers and what it does not. Is there a sin or struggle you've assumed is an unbreakable infinite chain, but maybe it's just a transient instrument that can be broken without needing to trace it back to a 'first cause'? What would it mean to treat it as such?
?
Consider your relationship with Allah. The ordered principles (causes) that lead to closeness to Him are clear: faith, prayer, sincerity. But do you focus more on the transient instruments (worldly distractions) that come and go, thinking they are part of an endless regress you cannot escape? How can you redirect your effort to the ordered principles that truly matter?

Answering the Objection of Events in Immaterial Entities
The author answers the objection that events require matter and time, thus cannot be in immaterial entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that successive events cannot occur in immaterial entities because they require matter and time, and how the author answers by referencing an earlier discussion.

Core Idea
Events need matter and time, but the author points to an earlier resolution.

In this passage, the author addresses a specific objection raised against the possibility of successive events in immaterial entities. The objector argues that every event requires matter and time, which are only found in corporeal things. The author's response is concise: he refers back to an earlier discussion where this point was already resolved. This lesson unpacks the objection and the method of the author's answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the conditional 'ولو سلم' and the structure of objection ('لا يقال') and answer ('لأنا نقول').
The passage is short but dense.
2
Identify the objection
Find the objector's claim: that succession of events is only valid for corporeal entities because every event is preceded by matter and time.
The objection uses a previously established principle.
3
Analyze the author's answer
The author says 'قد سبق الكلام على ذلك هنالك' — the discussion has already preceded there. What does this tell you about the author's method?
The author does not repeat the argument but trusts the reader to recall it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سلم فالكلام في العالم الجسماني فيجوز أن يكون حادثاً مستنداً الى حوادث متعاقبة لا أول لها كتصورات أو إرادات من ذات مجردة مثل ما ذكرتم في الحادث اليومي. لا يقال تعاقب الحوادث، إنما يصح في الجسمانيات دون المجردات لما سبق من أن كل حادث مسبوق بمادة ومدة . لأنا نقول : قد سبق الكلام على ذلك هنالك .
And if it is granted, then the discussion is about the corporeal world. It is possible that it is an originated thing based on successive events without beginning, such as concepts or volitions from an immaterial essence, similar to what you mentioned in the everyday originated event. It cannot be said that the succession of events is only valid for corporeal entities, not immaterial ones, because it has preceded that every event is preceded by matter and time. For we say: The discussion on that has already preceded there.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector grants the possibility and says: If we are discussing the corporeal world, it might be that it is originated from a chain of successive events without beginning, like concepts or volitions from an immaterial essence, as you mention for everyday events. The objection is that succession of events is only valid for corporeal entities, not immaterial ones, because every event requires matter and time. The author's answer is simply that this point has already been discussed earlier, referring to a previous treatment.

Third proof for the eternity of the world and its refutation
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the third proof from composition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' third proof for the eternity of the world based on composition of body, and how al-Taftazani refutes it by rejecting its premises.

Core Idea
The eternity of matter is argued from the impossibility of infinite regress.

This passage presents one of the philosophical proofs for the eternity of the world, based on the idea that every body is composed of matter and form. The philosophers argue that matter must be eternal to avoid an infinite regress, and since form is inseparable, the body itself is eternal. Al-Taftazani refutes this by simply rejecting the premises, noting that the underlying claims about hylemorphism have already been discussed and refuted earlier.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the overall argument and refutation.
Notice the concise style: the proof is summarized in a few lines, and the refutation is a single phrase.
2
Identify the proof's structure
Break down the proof: it has two premises (body composed of matter and form; matter is eternal due to no regress) and a conclusion (body is eternal).
The phrase 'li-imtina' tasalsul al-mawadd' is key for the second premise.
3
Understand the refutation
The author's response is 'man' al-muqaddimat' (the premises are rejected), and he refers to earlier discussion for a detailed refutation.
This shows that the author does not engage with the logic here but dismisses the foundational assumptions.
4
Reflect on the philosophical context
Consider why this proof relies on hylemorphism, a framework not accepted by the author, and why simply rejecting premises is a valid theological response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل ثالث على قدم العالم. قال (الثالث) والرد عليه: إن الجسم مركب من مادة هي قديمة لامتناع تسلسل المواد ومن صورة هي لازمة للمادة لما مرّ فيكون قديماً، والجواب: منع المقدمات. قد سبق الكلام على ما يدعيه الفلاسفة من تركب الجسم من الهيولي والصورة، وكون الهيولي قديمة وكونها غير منكفة عن صورة ما.
Third proof for the eternity of the world. He said: (The third) and the refutation of it: The body is composed of matter which is eternal due to the impossibility of a regression of matters, and of form which is necessary for matter as previously mentioned, so it is eternal. The answer: the premises are rejected. The discussion has already preceded on what the philosophers claim about the composition of body from hyle and form, and that the hyle is eternal, and that it is never free from some form.
Source pages p. 124

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof of the philosophers for the eternity of the world and the author's refutation. The proof argues that every body is composed of matter (hyle) and form. Matter is eternal because otherwise there would be an infinite regress of matters. Form is inseparable from matter. Thus body is eternal. The author replies by rejecting the premises, noting that the claim about composition and eternity of matter has already been discussed and refuted earlier.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is an independent logical argument that does not rely on any specific metaphysical framework.
Correction: The proof is based on hylemorphism, a philosophical framework that the author does not accept. The refutation works by rejecting these assumptions, not by disproving the logic.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why is matter claimed to be eternal?
A) Because it is composed of form
B) Because it is impossible for matter to be originated
C) Because otherwise there would be an infinite regress of matters
D) Because body is eternal

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof for the eternity of the world from composition is refuted by rejecting its premises, which are not accepted in the author's theological framework.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you ever accept arguments simply because they seem logical, without examining their foundational assumptions? How can you train yourself to question premises as this lesson does?
?
The philosophers built their proof on the idea that matter cannot be subject to infinite regress. What assumptions do you hold about the world that you have never critically examined? Will you examine them today?
?
The refutation here is decisive: 'the premises are rejected.' Are there beliefs you hold that you would be unwilling to reject even if their foundations were shown to be weak? What would it take for you to let them go?
