Variation of Day and Night with Latitude and Solar Position
Describes how day length varies with latitude and solar declination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the length of day and night varies with latitude and the sun's position in the zodiac, as described by the author to illustrate cosmic order.

Core Idea
The regular variation of day and night demonstrates cosmic order and divine wisdom.

This passage from a theological work sets out the astronomical principles governing the length of day and night. It explains why days are longer in summer and shorter in winter, and how this differs with latitude. Understanding this regularity is a step towards appreciating the order in creation that points to a wise Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own description come through.
The passage is dense with astronomical terminology; the translation will clarify.
2
Identify the two main variables
Notice that the author uses latitude (عرض) and the sun's position in the zodiac (being in northern or southern signs) as the key factors determining day length.
3
Observe the pattern with latitude
The author states that the difference between night and day increases with latitude. Look for the explanation involving visible and hidden poles.
This is a cause-and-effect relationship.
4
Trace the solstice progression
Follow the description of day increasing up to one solstice and decreasing to the other. This shows the cyclical nature.
The solstices are referred to as 'رأس المنقلب'.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why the author includes this detailed astronomical account in a theological work. It serves as evidence of order and wisdom in creation.
This connects to the broader argument for a wise Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في جنوب المعدل، كانت القسي الظاهرة أعظم في العرض الجنوبي، وأصغر في الشمالي، فعند كون الشمس في البروج الشمالية كان النهار أقصر في العرض الشمالي، وأطول في الجنوبي، وعند كونها في البروج الجنوبية كان الأمر بالعكس، وكلما كان عرض البلد أكثر كان مقدار التفاوت بين الليل والنهار أكثر لازدياد ارتفاع القطب الظاهر والمدارات التي تليه، وازدياد فضل قسيها الظاهرة على الخفية وازدياد انحطاط القطب الخفي، والمدارات التي عنده، فيزداد فضل قسيها الخفية على الظاهرة، ويكون تزايد النهار، وتناقص الليل إلى رأس المنقلب الذي يلي القطب الظاهر، وتناقص النهار، وتزايد الليل إلى رأس المنقلب الآخر، ويكون نهار كل جزء مساوياً لليل نظيره، وبالعكس كنهار أول السرطان لليل أول الجدي وبالعكس.
South of the equator, the visible arcs are greater in the southern latitude and smaller in the northern. Thus, when the sun is in the northern signs, the day is shorter in the northern latitude and longer in the southern; and when it is in the southern signs, the opposite occurs. The more the latitude of a country is, the more the amount of difference between night and day, due to the increase in altitude of the visible pole and the circles adjacent to it, and the increase in the excess of its visible arcs over the hidden ones, and the increase in depression of the hidden pole and the circles near it, so the excess of its hidden arcs over the visible ones increases. The day increases and night decreases up to the solstitial point next to the visible pole, and the day decreases and night increases up to the other solstitial point. The day of each part equals the night of its counterpart, and vice versa, like the day of the first of Cancer for the night of the first of Capricorn, and vice versa.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage is a scientific description of the variation in day length depending on latitude and the sun's position in the zodiac. The author explains that south of the equator, the visible arcs of the celestial sphere are larger in southern latitudes and smaller in northern latitudes. Consequently, when the sun is in northern signs (summer in the north), days are shorter in the north and longer in the south; the opposite occurs when the sun is in southern signs (winter in the north). The author then notes that the difference between night and day increases with latitude, due to the increasing altitude of the visible pole and the arcs around it, and the increasing depression of the hidden pole. This leads to a description of the progression from one solstice to another, where day and night lengthen or shorten correspondingly. Finally, the author states that the day of any part equals the night of its counterpart, giving the example of the first day of Cancer (summer solstice in the north) and the first day of Capricorn (winter solstice). This precise astronomical regularity serves as a background for the theological argument that follows, demonstrating order in creation that points to a wise creator.

قسي خفية
عرض
منقلب

Correction: The author uses this precise regularity as evidence for divine wisdom in creation, as the context of the theological work shows.

Philosophers on Celestial Colors: Objection and Response
Commentator presents the philosophers' error, an objection about colors, and their response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that celestial bodies lack color, the objection based on observed colors, and their response that these are illusions, and how this reveals the flaw in their necessitarian worldview.

Core Idea
Philosophers' denial of celestial colors masks deeper causal error.

This lesson examines a specific objection raised against philosophers who denied that celestial bodies have colors or qualities like heat and cold. The commentator shows that while the objection is based on direct observation—blue sky, red Mars, etc.—the philosophers' response is to explain these away as illusions or effects of light. This discussion highlights the deeper problem: their view of God as a necessitating cause rather than a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the philosophers' principle
Read the opening where the commentator explains that the philosophers' observations are correct but their foundation is corrupt: they consider the Maker a necessitating cause (موجب) not a free agent (مختار). This is the root of atheism (الإلحاد).
This principle leads them to deny inherent qualities in celestial bodies.
2
Identify the objection
The objection lists observable colors: the sky is blue, the moon at eclipse is black, Saturn is dim, Jupiter white, Mars red. Also, astrologers assign cold/dry to Saturn, hot/dry to Mars, etc.
This contradicts the claim that celestials are devoid of such qualities.
3
Examine the philosophers' response
They answer: blueness is imaginary in the air, not real in the sky; blackness of the moon is absence of light; differences in observed things are differences of light, not color. Planetary 'qualities' are effects of motions, not inherent.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the full response is cut off.
4
Connect to the broader critique
Notice how the commentator uses this example to show that the philosophers' physical explanations evade the real issue: their necessitarian framework leads to inconsistencies.
The objection and response are part of a larger argument against philosophical cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يريد أن أكثر ما ذكروا من عظم أمر السمويات وعجيب خلقها، وبديع صنعها، وانتظام أمرها أمر ممكن شهد به الأمارات، ودل عليه العلامات من غير إخلال بما ثبت من القواعد الشرعية، والعقائد الدينية، إلا أنهم بنوا ذلك على أصل هو كون الصانع موجباً لا مختاراً، وذلك في غاية الفساد [ وجعلوا له فرعاً هو تأثير الحركات والأوضاع فيما يظهر في عالم الكون والفساد ] من الحوادث، وهو أصل الإلحاد، ثم إنهم لما ذهبوا إلى أن الفلكيات خالية عن اللون والحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ونحو ذلك، أورد عليهم: أنا نشاهد السماء أزرق، والقمر عند الخسوف أسود، وزحل كمداً، والمشترى أبيض، والمريخ أحمر، وأنهم يجعلون زحل بارداً يابساً، والمريخ حاراً يابساً، وكذا في سائر السيارات، ودرجات البروج على ما بين في كتب الأحكام. فأجابوا بأن الزرقة متخيلة في الجو لا متحققة في السماء، وسواد القمر عدم إضاءة جرمه، وما يشاهد في المتحيزة ليس اختلاف ألوان، بل اختلاف أضواء . ومعنى وصف الكواكب أو الدرج بالكيفيات الفعلية والانفعالية ظهور تلك
He intends that most of what they mentioned about the greatness of heavenly matters, the wondrousness of their creation, the beauty of their craftsmanship, and the order of their affair is a possible matter attested by signs and indicated by tokens, without prejudice to what is established of legal principles and religious beliefs – except that they based that on a principle that the Maker is a necessitating cause not a free agent, and that is in extreme corruption. [And they made for it a branch: the influence of motions and positions on what appears in the world of generation and corruption] of events, and it is the root of atheism. Then, when they held that celestial bodies are devoid of color, heat, cold, moisture, dryness, and the like, an objection was raised against them: We see the sky blue, the moon at eclipse black, Saturn dim, Jupiter white, Mars red, and they make Saturn cold and dry, Mars hot and dry, and similarly for the other planets and degrees of the signs as explained in books of judgments. So they answered that blueness is imaginary in the air not actual in the sky, the blackness of the moon is absence of illumination of its body, and what is observed in extended things is not difference of colors but difference of lights. And the meaning of describing the planets or degrees with active and passive qualities is the appearance of those...
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's explanation and expansion of the original argument. The commentator clarifies that the philosophers' observations about the heavens are correct in themselves and do not contradict religious principles; the problem is that they attribute these to a necessitating cause rather than a free agent, which is corrupt and leads to atheism. The commentator then introduces an objection: if the celestial bodies are devoid of qualities like color, temperature, etc., why do we observe blue sky, black moon, red Mars, etc., and why do astrologers assign natures to planets? The philosophers respond that these are not real color or temperature differences: the sky's blueness is an optical illusion, the moon's blackness is absence of light, and planetary 'natures' are effects of motions, not inherent qualities. The passage ends mid-sentence, indicating a continuation.

الموجب
المختار
الإلحاد
الزرقة متخيلة
عدم إضاءة

Correction: The philosophers do have an explanation: they argue that colors are illusions or effects of light, not inherent to the celestial bodies. However, the commentator shows that this response is problematic because it undermines their own system.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' response to the color objection illustrates the tension between their empirical observations and their necessitarian causal framework, which forces them to reduce perceived qualities to illusions.

The commentator reveals that the philosophers' explanation, though coherent, actually undermines their own claims. Is there any belief or habit in your life that seems reasonable on the surface but contradicts deeper truths you hold about Allah? Are you willing to expose that inconsistency and realign your actions with your faith?

The Objection on Celestial Colors and the Philosophers' Response
Objection and philosophers' response on celestial colors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Islamic philosophers defended their view that celestial bodies are free from colors and qualities against empirical objections.

Core Idea
Observed celestial colors are illusions or light effects, not intrinsic properties.

This passage presents a key objection to the philosophers' claim that the heavens lack color and other qualities. The philosophers cleverly reinterpret what we see as optical illusions or the absence of light. Understanding this response is crucial for grasping the Islamic philosophical worldview on the celestial realm.

Guided Path
1
The philosophers' claim
Recall that philosophers hold celestial bodies to be free from qualities like color, heat, dryness. This passage defends that claim against an empirical objection.
2
The objection
The objection points to observable phenomena: the sky is blue, the moon turns black during eclipse, planets have distinct colors, and astrologers assign qualities like cold/dry to Saturn. This seems to contradict the philosophers' claim.
Notice how the objection uses both direct observation and astrological tradition.
3
The answer: reinterpreting observations
The philosophers respond by explaining each observation: blueness is an illusion in the air, the moon's blackness is absence of illumination, and what we see are differences in light, not colors. The assignment of qualities to planets refers to their effects on the sublunar world, not intrinsic properties.
This is a classic example of distinguishing real vs. apparent properties.
4
The underlying principle
Understand the key move: celestial bodies themselves are unchanging and free from qualities; any observed variability is due to our perspective, light, or causal effects in the lower world.
This preserves the immutability and perfection of the heavens.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم لما ذهبوا إلى أن الفلكيات خالية عن اللون والحرارة والبرودة والرطوبة واليبوسة ونحو ذلك، أورد عليهم أنا نشاهد السماء أزرق، والقمر عند الخسوف أسود، وزحل كمداً، والمشترى أبيض، والمريخ أحمر، وأنهم يجعلون زحل بارداً يابساً، والمريخ حاراً يابساً، وكذا في سائر السيارات، ودرجات البروج على ما بين في كتب الأحكام. فأجابوا بأن الزرقة متخيلة في الجولا متحققة في السماء، وسواد القمر عدم إضاءة جرمه، وما يشاهد في المتحيزة ليس اختلاف ألوان، بل اختلاف أضواء. ومعنى وصف الكواكب أو الدرج بالكيفيات الفعلية والانفعالية ظهور تلك الآثار سبباً في عالم العناصر بحسب ما يحدث لها من الحركات والأوضاع.
Then when they held that celestial bodies are free from color, heat, cold, moisture, dryness and the like, it was objected against them: we observe the sky blue, the moon at eclipse black, Saturn dusky, Jupiter white, Mars red; and they make Saturn cold and dry, Mars hot and dry, and likewise for the other planets and the degrees of the zodiac as explained in the books of judgments. So they answered that the blueness is imaginary in the air, not realized in the sky; the blackness of the moon is the absence of illumination of its body; and what is observed in extended bodies is not a difference of colors but a difference of lights. And the meaning of describing the planets or degrees with active and passive qualities is the appearance of those effects as a cause in the world of elements according to what occurs to them of movements and positions.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objection is empirical: we see the sky blue, the moon black during eclipse, planets with distinct colors, and astrologers assign qualities like cold/dry to Saturn and hot/dry to Mars—contradicting the claim that celestial bodies lack these qualities. The philosophers' answer is that the blueness is an illusion (mutakhayyila) in the air, not real in the sky; the moon's blackness is simply absence of light; what we perceive are differences in illumination, not intrinsic colors. The attribution of active (e.g., heat) and passive (e.g., moisture) qualities to planets and zodiac degrees refers not to inherent properties but to the effects these bodies produce in the sublunar world through their motions and positions. Thus the philosophers defend their view that celestial bodies themselves are free from such qualities.

Correction: They do not deny the phenomena; they reinterpret them as illusions or effects of light, not as intrinsic qualities of celestial bodies.

The Flaw in the Philosophers' Causal Argument
Refutes the philosophers' causal explanation for celestial differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical flaw in the philosophers' argument that similarities in celestial parts can be explained by active causes, and why it leads to either contradiction or the collapse of their system.

Core Idea
The philosophers' causal argument either fails to explain differences or admits a free agent, destroying their system.

This passage engages with a classic debate in Islamic theology: whether observed differences in the celestial spheres can be explained by the philosophers' necessitating causation. The author first presents the philosophers' objection and their attempted answer, then exposes a devastating critique that forces a choice between logical consistency and the core tenets of their worldview.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
The philosophers claim the celestial sphere is simple with no differentiating parts, yet they must assign specific roles to parts (ecliptic, pole, etc.). Notice the contradiction.
The objection arises from within the philosophers' own framework.
2
Examine the Philosophers' Response
They argue that while receptive causes (matter) are similar, active causes (movers) can differ, allowing for different effects.
This response attempts to reconcile uniformity with diversity.
3
Identify the Refutation
The author points out that if the agent is necessitating, its relation to all is equal, so differences cannot arise. If it is free, then the whole astronomical system built on denying a free agent collapses.
This is the decisive critique that exposes the flaw.
4
Grasp the Conclusion
The observed celestial differences may simply be due to divine will, undermining the philosophers' proof of specific motions and spheres.
This conclusion aligns with the Ash'ari doctrine of occasionalism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الآثار سبباً في عالم العناصر بحسب ما يحدث لها من الحركات والأوضاع، ولما ذهبوا إلى أن الفلك بسيط ليس فيه اختلاف أجزاء أصلاً، أورد عليهم تعين بعض الأجزاء لكونه منطقة، وبعضها لكونه قطباً، وبعضها لكونه محلاً لارتكاز الكواكب أو التدوير فيه إلى غير ذلك من الاختلافات اللازمة على أصولهم . فأجيب بأن تشابه الأسباب القابلية، لا ينافي اختلاف الآثار لجواز أن يكون عائداً إلى الأسباب الفاعلية، وفيه نظر لأن الفاعل إن كان موجباً كما هو مذهبهم، فنسبته إلى الكل على السواء، فلا يأتي هذا الاختلاف. وإن كان مختاراً كما هو الحق، فقد سقط جميع ما بنوا من أصول علم الهيئة على نفي الفاعل المختار، إذ يجوز أن يكون اختلاف الحركات والأوضاع المشاهدة مستنداً إلى مشيئة القادر المختار، فلا يثبت ما أثبتوا من الحركات والأفلاك .
The effects as a cause in the elemental world according to what occurs to them of motions and states. When they held that the celestial sphere is simple with no difference of parts at all, they were objected to regarding the determination of some parts as the ecliptic, some as the pole, some as a place for the fixing of planets or the epicycle, etc. of differences necessitated by their principles. It was answered that the similarity of receptive causes does not preclude difference of effects, since it may be attributed to active causes. But this is questionable, because if the agent is necessitating—as is their doctrine—then its relation to all is equal, so this difference cannot occur. And if it is a free agent—as is the truth—then all that they have built from the principles of astronomy on the denial of the free agent collapses, for it is possible that the observed differences of motions and states are based on the will of the powerful chooser, and thus what they posited of motions and spheres is not proven.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and refutation. First, it states the objection: the philosophers claim the celestial sphere is simple with no parts, yet they assign specific parts like the ecliptic, pole, and planetary locations. The philosophers answer that the receptive causes (the matter of the sphere) are uniform, but the active causes (the movers) differ, so differences in effects are possible. The refutation then shows the flaw: if the active agent acts by necessity, its relation to all parts is equal, so differentiation cannot arise. But if the agent acts by choice (which is the truth), then the entire astronomical system built on denying a free agent is invalid, because the observed differences could simply be due to divine will. Thus, the specific motions and spheres are not proven from their own assumptions.

Key Terms

موجب
علم الهيئة
حركات
أفلاك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' distinction between receptive and active causes satisfactorily explains celestial differences.
Correction: The author shows that if the active agent is necessitating, it cannot produce differentiation; if it is free, it undermines the philosophers' entire astronomical system.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The philosophers' entire system collapsed because it denied the free agent. Where in your own life—your career, relationships, or spiritual habits—are you relying on impersonal systems or sheer routine, neglecting that Allah is the active Chooser over every detail?

The Inconsistency of Voluntary and Eternal Celestial Motions
Raises an objection: celestial voluntary motions should be variable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that celestial motions are both voluntary and eternal is internally inconsistent.

Core Idea
Voluntary motion cannot be forced to be eternal and uniform.

The philosophers' attempt to harmonize celestial mechanics with voluntary agency leads to a logical tension. This passage presents a crisp objection: if these motions arise from will, they should admit variation and cessation, not eternal uniformity. Let's see how the author drives this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence carefully. Identify the two key claims: (1) the philosophers say these motions are voluntary and from particular wills, (2) they are necessarily eternal and uniform.
The objection builds on an upcoming discussion about celestial souls.
2
Translate and understand
Read the English translation. Notice the contrast between 'voluntary motions' and 'necessitated eternally'. The word 'مع' (while) sets up the tension.
3
Pinpoint the contradiction
The objection states that voluntary motion can differ or cease according to will. But the philosophers hold these motions to be permanent and identical forever. That is the inconsistency.
Think about human voluntary actions—they can change.
4
Consider the implication
If the philosophers were consistent, they would have to admit either that celestial motions are not truly voluntary or that they can change. They cannot have both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم عليهم اعتراض آخر، وهو أنهم جعلوا هذه الحركات المخصوصة على النظام المخصوص مع لزومها أزلاً وأبداً من قبيل الحركات الإرادية واقعة بإرادات جزئية من النفوس الفلكية على ما سيأتي مع أنا قاطعون بأن الحركة الإرادية يجوز أن تختلف أو تنقطع بمقتضى الإرادة، ولا يلزم أن تستمر على وتيرة واحدة .
Then there is another objection, namely that they made these particular motions on this particular system while they are necessitated eternally and forever, from the category of voluntary motions, occurring by particular wills from the celestial souls as will come, while we are certain that voluntary motion may differ or cease according to will, and it is not necessary that it continues on one pattern.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a straightforward objection: the philosophers claim that celestial motions are voluntary, originating from particular wills of celestial souls. Yet they also assert that these motions are eternal and uniform. The objection points out that voluntary motions by nature can differ or stop, so there is no necessity for them to continue forever on the same pattern. Thus, the philosophers' position is internally inconsistent.

Key Terms

نفوس فلكية
إرادات جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think celestial motions are special and not subject to normal rules of voluntary motion.
Correction: The author treats them as analogous to human voluntary actions, so they must admit variation and cessation.

Checkpoint

A) They claim motions are voluntary yet eternal and uniform
B) They claim motions are necessitated yet changeable
C) They claim souls are material
D) They claim only one mover

Key Takeaway
Voluntary motion, by nature, can vary or cease; thus, claiming celestial motions are both voluntary and eternal is logically inconsistent.

The Four Elements: Definitions and Empirical Proof
Definition and empirical proof of the four elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the four classical elements are defined by pairs of primary qualities and how empirical reasoning and induction support this classification.

Core Idea
The four elements are uniquely defined by combining two of the four primary qualities: heat, cold, moisture, and dryness.

In this passage, the author presents the foundational doctrine of the four elements as held by the philosophers. He first states the elements—fire, air, water, and earth—then provides the key argument from sensory evidence and induction that leads to exactly four elemental simples. This lesson focuses on the definitions and the main empirical proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the author's flow: he states the elements, gives evidence, argues for two qualities, and then defines each element.
2
Identify the four elements and their qualities
From the passage, list each element and the two qualities that define it. Check that fire is hot+dry, air is hot+moist, water is cold+moist, earth is cold+dry.
3
Understand the empirical argument
The passage states that sensory evidence shows all elemental bodies contain the four qualities. No simple has only one, and four or three cannot combine due to opposition. This forces two qualities per element.
This is the proof from composition and decomposition.
4
Reflect on the role of induction
The passage begins by saying reliance is on induction (استقراء). Notice that the proof is not purely rational but grounded in observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهواء، وبارد رطب هو الماء وبارد يابس هو الأرض، وبنوا طرق الحصر على هذه الكيفيات الأربع، ولوازمها، مثل الخفة والثقل على الإطلاق أو الإضافة، ومثل الاجماع والافتراق بسهولة أو عسر، والتعويل على الاستقراء المعول عليه من أقوال الفلاسفة، أنها أربعة النار والهواء والماء والأرض، لأن الشواهد الحسية والتجربة والتأمل في أحوال التركيبات والتحليلات، قد دلت على أن الأجسام العنصرية بسائطها ومركباتها لا تخلو عن حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة، ولم يوجد في البسائط ما يشتمل على واحدة فقط، ولم يمكن اجتماع الأربعة أو الثلاثة لما بين الحرارة والبرودة، وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة من التضاد. فتعين اجتماع اثنتين من الكيفيات الأربع في كل بسيط عنصري، فالجامع بين الحرارة واليبوسة هو النار، وبين الحرارة والرطوبة هو الهواء، وبين البرودة والرطوبة هو الماء، وبين البرودة واليبوسة هو الأرض، ومبنى ما ذكروا في بيان الحصر على هذه الكيفيات الأربع كما يقال: العنصر إما حار أو بارد، وكل منهما إما يابس أو رطب، أو على لوازمها. كما يقال العنصر إما خفيف أو ثقيل، وكل منهما إما على الإطلاق أو على الإضافة، أو يقال لا بد في تركيب الممتزجات من لطيف أو كثيف. فاللطيف إما بحيث يحرق ما يلاقيه وهو النار أو لا وهو الهواء، والكثيف إما سيال وهو الماء أو لا وهو الأرض. أو يقال لا بد من قبول الأشكال، وجمع وتفريق للأجزاء، فالعنصر إما قابل للأشكال بسهولة، أو بعسر، وكل منهما إما أن يكون له قوة جامعة أو مفرقة. هذا والتعويل على الاستقراء، ولابن سينا في ذلك كلام طويل أورده الإمام في المباحث مع جمل من الاعتراضات عليه. ثم قال: والحق أن من حاول بيان الحصر للعناصر بتقسيم عقلي، فقد حاول ما لا يمكنه الوفاء به. نعم الناس لما بحثوا بطريق التركيب والتحليل، وجدوا تركيب الكائنات مبتدأ من هذه الأربعة، وتحليلها منتهياً إليها. فلاجرم زعموا أن الاستقسات هي هذه الأربعة.
Air, and cold-moist is water and cold-dry is earth, and they built the methods of enumeration on these four qualities, and their necessary concomitants, such as lightness and heaviness absolutely or relatively, and such as gathering and separating with ease or difficulty, and reliance on induction. What is relied upon from the statements of the philosophers is that they are four: fire, air, water, and earth, because sensory evidence, experience, and reflection on the states of compositions and decompositions have indicated that elemental bodies, their simple and compound forms, are not devoid of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, and no simple was found that includes only one, and it was not possible for four or three to combine because of the opposition between heat and cold, and between moisture and dryness. So it was determined that two of the four qualities combine in every elemental simple; the unifier of heat and dryness is fire, of heat and moisture is air, of cold and moisture is water, and of cold and dryness is earth. The basis of what they mentioned in explaining the enumeration is on these four qualities, as it is said: the element is either hot or cold, and each of them is either dry or moist, or on their necessary concomitants. As it is said: the element is either light or heavy, and each of them is either absolutely or relatively, or it is said: in the composition of mixtures, there must be either subtle or dense. The subtle is either such that it burns what it meets – that is fire – or not – that is air; the dense is either fluid – that is water – or not – that is earth. Or it is said: there must be acceptance of shapes, and gathering and separation of parts; the element is either easily receptive of shapes or with difficulty, and each of them either has a uniting or separating force. This, and reliance on induction, and Ibn Sina has a long discussion on that, which the Imam mentioned in al-Mabahith along with a number of objections against it. Then he said: The truth is that whoever attempts to demonstrate the enumeration of elements by rational division has attempted what he cannot fulfill. Rather, when people investigated by way of composition and decomposition, they found that the composition of beings begins from these four, and their decomposition ends with them. Hence, they supposed that the elements are these four.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the four elements as held by the philosophers. It begins by summarizing the classification: hot-dry is fire, hot-moist is air, cold-moist is water, cold-dry is earth. The basis of enumeration is on the four qualities and their concomitants. Then it provides the main proof: sensory evidence and experience show that all elemental bodies are composed of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. No simple body has only one quality, and four or three cannot combine due to opposition, so exactly two qualities combine in each simple element. This yields the four combinations corresponding to the four elements. The passage then presents alternative ways of enumerating based on qualities or concomitants like lightness/heaviness, subtle/dense, etc. It emphasizes that the reliance is on induction (استقراء), not purely rational division. It notes that Ibn Sina discussed this at length, and the Imam reported his discussion with objections. The passage concludes with the truth: a rational division cannot definitively prove the four elements; rather, induction from composition and decomposition in nature leads to the conclusion that they are four.

Key Takeaway
The four classical elements are empirically derived from the combination of two primary qualities, and no other combination is possible due to opposition.

Alternative Enumeration Methods and the Role of Induction
Alternative enumeration methods and emphasis on induction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative rational methods used to enumerate the four elements and why the author concludes that only induction, not rational division, provides a valid proof.

Core Idea
The four elements can be enumerated through various rational divisions, but the only sound proof is induction from composition and decomposition.

This passage continues by presenting several alternative ways to enumerate the four elements, using distinctions like hot/cold, light/heavy, subtle/dense, and acceptance of shapes. However, the author emphasizes that all these methods ultimately depend on induction, and he quotes Ibn Sina and al-Imam al-Razi to caution against relying solely on rational division. This lesson explores these alternative methods and the epistemological status of the four elements.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice that it presents multiple classification schemes (by qualities, by lightness, by subtlety, by shape acceptance).
2
Identify the alternative methods
List each method: (1) hot/cold then dry/moist; (2) light/heavy absolutely or relatively; (3) subtle/dense with burning/fluidity; (4) ease of shape acceptance with gathering/separation force.
Each yields the same four elements.
3
Understand the critique of rational division
Note the statement that whoever attempts to demonstrate the enumeration by rational division cannot fulfill it. The correct method is induction from observation of composition and decomposition.
4
Reflect on the role of Ibn Sina and the Imam
The passage references Ibn Sina's long discussion and the Imam's objections, showing scholarly debate. But the final truth is that induction is the basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الهواء، وبارد رطب هو الماء وبارد يابس هو الأرض، وبنوا طرق الحصر على هذه الكيفيات الأربع، ولوازمها، مثل الخفة والثقل على الإطلاق أو الإضافة، ومثل الاجماع والافتراق بسهولة أو عسر، والتعويل على الاستقراء المعول عليه من أقوال الفلاسفة، أنها أربعة النار والهواء والماء والأرض، لأن الشواهد الحسية والتجربة والتأمل في أحوال التركيبات والتحليلات، قد دلت على أن الأجسام العنصرية بسائطها ومركباتها لا تخلو عن حرارة وبرودة ورطوبة ويبوسة، ولم يوجد في البسائط ما يشتمل على واحدة فقط، ولم يمكن اجتماع الأربعة أو الثلاثة لما بين الحرارة والبرودة، وبين الرطوبة واليبوسة من التضاد. فتعين اجتماع اثنتين من الكيفيات الأربع في كل بسيط عنصري، فالجامع بين الحرارة واليبوسة هو النار، وبين الحرارة والرطوبة هو الهواء، وبين البرودة والرطوبة هو الماء، وبين البرودة واليبوسة هو الأرض، ومبنى ما ذكروا في بيان الحصر على هذه الكيفيات الأربع كما يقال: العنصر إما حار أو بارد، وكل منهما إما يابس أو رطب، أو على لوازمها. كما يقال العنصر إما خفيف أو ثقيل، وكل منهما إما على الإطلاق أو على الإضافة، أو يقال لا بد في تركيب الممتزجات من لطيف أو كثيف. فاللطيف إما بحيث يحرق ما يلاقيه وهو النار أو لا وهو الهواء، والكثيف إما سيال وهو الماء أو لا وهو الأرض. أو يقال لا بد من قبول الأشكال، وجمع وتفريق للأجزاء، فالعنصر إما قابل للأشكال بسهولة، أو بعسر، وكل منهما إما أن يكون له قوة جامعة أو مفرقة. هذا والتعويل على الاستقراء، ولابن سينا في ذلك كلام طويل أورده الإمام في المباحث مع جمل من الاعتراضات عليه. ثم قال: والحق أن من حاول بيان الحصر للعناصر بتقسيم عقلي، فقد حاول ما لا يمكنه الوفاء به. نعم الناس لما بحثوا بطريق التركيب والتحليل، وجدوا تركيب الكائنات مبتدأ من هذه الأربعة، وتحليلها منتهياً إليها. فلاجرم زعموا أن الاستقسات هي هذه الأربعة.
Air, and cold-moist is water and cold-dry is earth, and they built the methods of enumeration on these four qualities, and their necessary concomitants, such as lightness and heaviness absolutely or relatively, and such as gathering and separating with ease or difficulty, and reliance on induction. What is relied upon from the statements of the philosophers is that they are four: fire, air, water, and earth, because sensory evidence, experience, and reflection on the states of compositions and decompositions have indicated that elemental bodies, their simple and compound forms, are not devoid of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness, and no simple was found that includes only one, and it was not possible for four or three to combine because of the opposition between heat and cold, and between moisture and dryness. So it was determined that two of the four qualities combine in every elemental simple; the unifier of heat and dryness is fire, of heat and moisture is air, of cold and moisture is water, and of cold and dryness is earth. The basis of what they mentioned in explaining the enumeration is on these four qualities, as it is said: the element is either hot or cold, and each of them is either dry or moist, or on their necessary concomitants. As it is said: the element is either light or heavy, and each of them is either absolutely or relatively, or it is said: in the composition of mixtures, there must be either subtle or dense. The subtle is either such that it burns what it meets – that is fire – or not – that is air; the dense is either fluid – that is water – or not – that is earth. Or it is said: there must be acceptance of shapes, and gathering and separation of parts; the element is either easily receptive of shapes or with difficulty, and each of them either has a uniting or separating force. This, and reliance on induction, and Ibn Sina has a long discussion on that, which the Imam mentioned in al-Mabahith along with a number of objections against it. Then he said: The truth is that whoever attempts to demonstrate the enumeration of elements by rational division has attempted what he cannot fulfill. Rather, when people investigated by way of composition and decomposition, they found that the composition of beings begins from these four, and their decomposition ends with them. Hence, they supposed that the elements are these four.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof of the four elements as held by the philosophers. It begins by summarizing the classification: hot-dry is fire, hot-moist is air, cold-moist is water, cold-dry is earth. The basis of enumeration is on the four qualities and their concomitants. Then it provides the main proof: sensory evidence and experience show that all elemental bodies are composed of heat, cold, moisture, and dryness. No simple body has only one quality, and four or three cannot combine due to opposition, so exactly two qualities combine in each simple element. This yields the four combinations corresponding to the four elements. The passage then presents alternative ways of enumerating based on qualities or concomitants like lightness/heaviness, subtle/dense, etc. It emphasizes that the reliance is on induction (استقراء), not purely rational division. It notes that Ibn Sina discussed this at length, and the Imam reported his discussion with objections. The passage concludes with the truth: a rational division cannot definitively prove the four elements; rather, induction from composition and decomposition in nature leads to the conclusion that they are four.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The four elements can be proven by pure rational division without empirical input.
Correction: The passage states that rational division cannot fulfill the proof; the correct method is induction from composition and decomposition.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the truth regarding the enumeration of elements?
A) It can be proven by any rational method mentioned.
B) It relies solely on the method of hot/cold.
C) It is based on induction from composition and decomposition.
D) It is an arbitrary classification with no proof.

Key Takeaway
While various rational divisions can enumerate the four elements, the only valid proof is induction from the composition and decomposition of bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah's attributes, do you trust neat logical systems more than the evidence of His creation and revelation? What is one attribute you can better understand by observing the world around you today?
?
The passage shows that rational schemes can be elegant but unreliable. Can you identify a belief in your own life—religious or otherwise—that you hold because it 'makes sense' logically, but you have never tested against reality? Will you seek real evidence for it this week?
?
If the scholars had to humble their rational pride and rely on induction, where in your spiritual life are you being called to set aside your own clever reasoning and submit to what Scripture and creation actually show you?

Doubts and Disagreements on the Four Elements
Discusses doubts about the sphere of fire and varying opinions on the number of elements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific doubts raised about the four-element theory and how classical scholars addressed alternative philosophical opinions.

Core Idea
Not all doubts are equal—only three specific issues challenge the four-element theory.

In this passage, the author examines the strongest objections against the classical theory of four elements. He acknowledges that philosophical disagreements exist but shows that only a few doubts carry real weight. We will explore those doubts and see how alternative views are dismissed for lacking solid evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get an overall sense of the argument by reading the Arabic text and its English translation once.
Notice the passage is cut off; it only fully introduces the first doubt.
2
Identify the three matters of doubt
Look for where the author lists the three matters that cause doubt. The first is the existence of the sphere of fire.
The passage ends before listing the second and third matters.
3
Understand why other opinions are rejected
The author mentions various philosophical opinions on the number of elements but dismisses them because they lack evidence that contradicts correct induction.
These opinions are not considered valid doubts.
4
Reflect on the strength of evidence
Consider why inference from effects (like meteors) is weak due to possible alternative causes.
This weakens the case for the sphere of fire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ولم يقو الاشتباه) إلا في وجود كرة النار ثم في يبوستها، وفي حرارة الهواء، والاستدلال بالآثار ضعيف لإمكان أسباب أخر، وبأن النار لو كانت حارة رطبة لكانت هواء، والهواء بارداً رطباً لكان ماء أضعف لإمكان الاشتراك في اللوازم سيما المختلفة بالشدة والضعف. يعني أن للفلاسفة في كمية العناصر اختلافات منهم من جعل العنصر واحداً، والبواقي بالاستحالة، قيل النار، وقيل الهواء، وقيل الماء، وقيل الأرض، وقيل البخار، ومنهم من جعله اثنين. قيل النار والأرض، وقيل الماء والأرض، وقيل الهواء والأرض، ومنهم من جعله ثلاثة قيل النار والهواء والأرض، وإنما الماء هواء متكاثف، وقيل الهواء والماء والأرض، وإنما النار هواء شديد الحرارة، ولم يذكروا لهذه الأقوال شبهة تعارض الاستقرار الصحيح، فتدفع ظن كون العناصر أربعة على الكيفيات المذكورة ولم يقو الاشتباه إلا في أمور ثلاثة: الأول وجود كرة النار فإنه لا سبيل إلى إثباتها، والاستدلال بالشهب،
He said: 'And doubt is not strong except concerning the existence of the sphere of fire, then its dryness, and concerning the heat of air. And inference from effects is weak because of the possibility of other causes, and that if fire were hot-moist it would be air, and if air were cold-moist it would be water — weaker because of the possibility of sharing in concomitants, especially those differing in intensity and weakness.' Meaning that the philosophers have differences regarding the number of elements: some made the element one, and the rest by transformation — it was said fire, said air, said water, said earth, said vapor; and some made it two — it was said fire and earth, water and earth, air and earth; and some made it three — it was said fire, air, and earth, and water is dense air; and it was said air, water, and earth, and fire is intensely hot air. They did not mention for these sayings any doubt that contradicts correct induction, so that repels the suspicion that the elements are four based on the mentioned qualities. And doubt is not strong except in three matters: first, the existence of the sphere of fire, for there is no way to prove it, and inference by meteors...
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer discussion regarding the four-element theory. It begins by quoting the text (likely from al-Maqasid) that the only strong doubts are about three matters: the existence of the sphere of fire, its dryness, and the heat of air. The inference from effects (like meteors for fire's sphere) is weak because other causes are possible. The argument that if fire were hot-moist it would be air, etc., is even weaker because properties can be shared with varying intensity. The commentator then explains that philosophers have many opinions on the number of elements: some say one, others two (in various pairs), others three, with explanations like water being dense air or fire being intensely hot air. None of these opinions provide a genuine doubt that contradicts correct induction, so they do not undermine the four-element theory based on the stated qualities. The only real doubts are the three listed, the first being the existence of the sphere of fire, which cannot be proven, and the evidence from meteors is insufficient. The passage cuts off here, indicating the enumeration is incomplete.

Correction: The passage shows that disagreements lack solid evidence and do not contradict correct induction, so they do not refute the theory.

Only three specific doubts about the four elements have any strength, and alternative opinions lack convincing proof.

Refutation of the Doubt about the Existence of the Sphere of Fire
The author refutes the philosophical doubt about the existence of a sphere of fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the existence of the sphere of fire cannot be rationally proven and how alternative explanations weaken the philosophical argument.

Core Idea
The sphere of fire is not provable; celestial phenomena have plausible non-fiery causes.

The author addresses the first of three doubts about the elemental spheres: the claim that there exists a sphere of pure fire surrounding the air. Philosophers inferred its existence from meteors, but the author shows this inference is weak because meteors could have other causes, especially on Islamic principles. In this lesson, we examine this refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the first doubt
Read the Arabic text and translation focusing on the arguments about the sphere of fire. Notice how the author critiques the inference from meteors.
2
Identify the philosophical claim
The philosophers claim there is a sphere of fire based on meteors. Find that claim in the passage.
3
Understand the refutation
The author says the inference is weak because other causes are possible, especially on Islamic principles. He suggests what we see as fire could be hot air.
4
Reflect on methodological principle
This shows the principle that inference from effects is weak when alternative explanations exist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال (ولم يقو الاشتباه) إلا في وجود كرة النار ثم في يبوستها، وفي حرارة الهواء، والاستدلال بالآثار ضعيف لإمكان أسباب أخر ، وبأن النار لو كانت حارة رطبة لكانت هواء، والهواء بارداً رطباً لكان ماء أضعف لإمكان الاشتراك في اللوازم سيما المختلفة بالشدة والضعف ] . يعني أن للفلاسفة في كمية العناصر اختلافات منهم من جعل العنصر واحداً، والبواقي بالاستحالة، قيل النار ، وقيل الهواء، وقيل الماء، وقيل الأرض، وقيل البخار، ومنهم من جعله اثنين. قيل النار والأرض، وقيل الماء والأرض، وقيل الهواء والأرض ، ومنهم من جعله ثلاثة قيل النار والهواء والأرض، وإنما الماء هواء متكاثف، وقيل الهواء والماء والأرض ، وإنما النار هواء شديد الحرارة ، ولم يذكروا لهذه الأقوال شبهة تعارض الاستقرار الصحيح، فتدفع ظن كون العناصر أربعة على الكيفيات المذكورة ولم يقو الاشتباه إلا في أمور ثلاثة : الأول وجود كرة النار فإنه لا سبيل إلى إثباتها، والاستدلال بالشهب زعماً منهم أنها دخان غليظ، يشتعل بالوصول إلى كرة النار ضعيف لجواز أن يكون لها سبب غير ذلك، سيما على القواعد الإسلامية، وأن يكون ما يشاهد من الشعل والنيران هواء اشتدت حرارته لا عنصراً برأسه . ذلك هو الثاني : يبوسة النار بمعنى عسر قبول التشكلات وتركها . فإن الطريق إلى أمثال التجربة والمشاهدة ولا مجال لهما في النار الصرفة المحيطة بالهواء على زعمهم ، وأما المخلوطة التي على وجه الأرض فظاهر أنها بخلاف ذلك، والاستدلال بأن شأن الحرارة إفناء الرطوبات، والنار الصرفة في غاية الحرارة، فيلزم أن تكون في غاية اليبوسة ضعيف، لأن إفناءها للرطوبة الطبيعية المفسرة بسهولة قبول التشكيلات وتركها غير مسلم، بل إنما تعنى البلة والأجزاء المائية وما هو كذلك، لا يلزم أن يكون يابساً في نفسه كما في الهواء الصرف. الثالث : حرارة الهواء فإنه لا دليل على إثباتها في الهواء الصرف، عن انعكاس الأشعة، ألا ترى أنه كلما كان أرفع من الأرض كان أبرد إلى أن يصير زمهريراً. يقال إن ذلك بسبب مجاورة الباردين. أعني الماء والأرض مع زوال المانع . أعني انعكاس الأشعة، فغير مسلّم . وأما الاستدلال بأن النار حارة، فلو كانت رطبـة لكانت هواء، وبأن الهواء رطب، فلو كان بارداً لكان ماء ففي غاية الضعف لأن الاشتراك في اللوازم سيما اللوازم المختلفة بالشدة والضعف المختص بكل من الملزومات بعض تلك المختلفات لا يوجب اتحاد الملزومات في الماهية .
[He said: And doubt does not gain strength except regarding the existence of the sphere of fire, then regarding its dryness, and regarding the heat of air. And inference from effects is weak because other causes are possible, and (the argument) that if fire were hot and moist it would be air, and if air were cold and moist it would be water, is weaker because of the possibility of sharing in concomitants, especially those differing in intensity and weakness.] That is, the philosophers have differences concerning the number of elements. Some make the element one, the rest by transformation—some say fire, some say air, some say water, some say earth, some say vapor. Some make it two—some say fire and earth, some say water and earth, some say air and earth. Some make it three—some say fire, air, and earth, water being condensed air; some say air, water, and earth, fire being intensely hot air. They did not mention for these sayings any plausible proof that contradicts the correct conviction (al-istiqrār al-ṣaḥīḥ), so they would repel the opinion that the elements are four with the mentioned qualities. And doubt does not gain strength except in three matters: First, the existence of the sphere of fire—there is no way to prove it. The inference from meteors, claiming they are thick smoke that ignites upon reaching the sphere of fire, is weak because it is possible that it has another cause, especially on Islamic principles, and that what is seen of flames and fires is air that has become intensely hot, not a separate element. That is it. Second, the dryness of fire, meaning difficulty of accepting forms and leaving them. The way to such things is experiment and observation, but there is no place for them in the pure fire surrounding the air according to their claim. As for the mixed fire on the surface of the earth, it is obviously contrary to that. And the inference that the property of heat is to destroy moistures, and pure fire is extremely hot, so it follows that it is extremely dry, is weak, because its destruction of natural moisture (interpreted as ease of accepting and leaving forms) is not conceded. Rather, it only means wetness and watery parts, and what is like that; it does not necessitate that it is dry in itself, as in pure air. Third, the heat of air—there is no proof for it in pure air, apart from the reflection of rays. Do you not see that the higher it is from the earth, the colder it becomes until it becomes freezing? It is said that this is due to adjacency to the two cold things, that is, water and earth, with the removal of the impediment, that is, the reflection of rays, but this is not conceded. As for the inference that fire is hot, so if it were moist it would be air, and that air is moist, so if it were cold it would be water, it is extremely weak, because sharing in concomitants, especially concomitants differing in intensity and weakness specific to each of the things entailed, does not necessitate union of the things entailed in quiddity.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three philosophical doubts concerning the elemental spheres. The author first quotes the matn stating that doubt only gains strength in three matters: the existence of the sphere of fire, its dryness, and the heat of air. The matn also notes that inference from effects is weak due to alternative explanations, and the argument from necessary properties is weaker because of possible shared concomitants. The commentary then explains that earlier philosophers disagree on the number of elements (one, two, three, or four) but these alternatives lack probative force. The commentary then addresses each doubt in turn. First doubt: the existence of a sphere of fire cannot be proven; the argument from meteors (shuhub) is weak because meteors could be due to other causes, and on Islamic principles what we see as fire may be intensely hot air, not a distinct element. Second doubt: the dryness of fire (meaning resistance to shaping) is not empirically verifiable for pure fire; earthly fire is not dry. The argument that extreme heat destroys moisture and thus implies extreme dryness is rejected because 'destruction of moisture' in this context does not mean the fire itself is dry; it only removes wetness without making fire dry. Third doubt: the heat of pure air is not proven; the observation that air gets colder at higher altitudes contradicts the claim. The counterargument that this is due to adjacency to cold bodies and removal of the heat-reflecting barrier is not conceded. The argument that if fire were moist it would be air, and if air were cold it would be water, is weak because shared concomitants (especially varying in intensity) do not imply identity of essence. Thus all three doubts are refuted.

كرة النار
الشهب
الاستدلال بالآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Meteors prove a sphere of fire exists.
Correction: Meteors could be caused by other phenomena, and Islamic principles allow alternative explanations.

Key Takeaway
The existence of the sphere of fire is unproven; what is observed as fire could be intensely heated air.

Refutation of the Doubt about the Dryness of Fire
The author refutes the philosophical doubt about the dryness of fire.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that pure fire is dry is empirically unverifiable and logically weak.

Core Idea
Dryness of fire is not proven; empirical evidence is lacking and the logical inference is flawed.

The second doubt concerns the dryness of the sphere of fire, i.e., its resistance to shaping. The philosophers argued that extreme heat destroys moisture, making fire extremely dry. The author counters that this is not empirically verifiable for pure fire and that the inference misunderstands what 'moisture' means. In this lesson, we examine the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on dryness of fire
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the author distinguishes between pure fire (allegedly surrounding the air) and mixed fire on earth.
2
Understand the philosophical argument
Philosophers argue: extreme heat destroys moisture, so pure fire must be dry. Find this in the text.
3
See the refutation
The author says: (1) we have no empirical access to pure fire, (2) destruction of moisture does not make the fire itself dry, as seen in air.
4
Reflect on the methodological lesson
This shows the importance of empirical evidence and careful definition of terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال (ولم يقو الاشتباه) إلا في وجود كرة النار ثم في يبوستها، وفي حرارة الهواء، والاستدلال بالآثار ضعيف لإمكان أسباب أخر ، وبأن النار لو كانت حارة رطبة لكانت هواء، والهواء بارداً رطباً لكان ماء أضعف لإمكان الاشتراك في اللوازم سيما المختلفة بالشدة والضعف ] . يعني أن للفلاسفة في كمية العناصر اختلافات منهم من جعل العنصر واحداً، والبواقي بالاستحالة، قيل النار ، وقيل الهواء، وقيل الماء، وقيل الأرض، وقيل البخار، ومنهم من جعله اثنين. قيل النار والأرض، وقيل الماء والأرض، وقيل الهواء والأرض ، ومنهم من جعله ثلاثة قيل النار والهواء والأرض، وإنما الماء هواء متكاثف، وقيل الهواء والماء والأرض ، وإنما النار هواء شديد الحرارة ، ولم يذكروا لهذه الأقوال شبهة تعارض الاستقرار الصحيح، فتدفع ظن كون العناصر أربعة على الكيفيات المذكورة ولم يقو الاشتباه إلا في أمور ثلاثة : الأول وجود كرة النار فإنه لا سبيل إلى إثباتها، والاستدلال بالشهب زعماً منهم أنها دخان غليظ، يشتعل بالوصول إلى كرة النار ضعيف لجواز أن يكون لها سبب غير ذلك، سيما على القواعد الإسلامية، وأن يكون ما يشاهد من الشعل والنيران هواء اشتدت حرارته لا عنصراً برأسه . ذلك هو الثاني : يبوسة النار بمعنى عسر قبول التشكلات وتركها . فإن الطريق إلى أمثال التجربة والمشاهدة ولا مجال لهما في النار الصرفة المحيطة بالهواء على زعمهم ، وأما المخلوطة التي على وجه الأرض فظاهر أنها بخلاف ذلك، والاستدلال بأن شأن الحرارة إفناء الرطوبات، والنار الصرفة في غاية الحرارة، فيلزم أن تكون في غاية اليبوسة ضعيف، لأن إفناءها للرطوبة الطبيعية المفسرة بسهولة قبول التشكيلات وتركها غير مسلم، بل إنما تعنى البلة والأجزاء المائية وما هو كذلك، لا يلزم أن يكون يابساً في نفسه كما في الهواء الصرف. الثالث : حرارة الهواء فإنه لا دليل على إثباتها في الهواء الصرف، عن انعكاس الأشعة، ألا ترى أنه كلما كان أرفع من الأرض كان أبرد إلى أن يصير زمهريراً. يقال إن ذلك بسبب مجاورة الباردين. أعني الماء والأرض مع زوال المانع . أعني انعكاس الأشعة، فغير مسلّم . وأما الاستدلال بأن النار حارة، فلو كانت رطبـة لكانت هواء، وبأن الهواء رطب، فلو كان بارداً لكان ماء ففي غاية الضعف لأن الاشتراك في اللوازم سيما اللوازم المختلفة بالشدة والضعف المختص بكل من الملزومات بعض تلك المختلفات لا يوجب اتحاد الملزومات في الماهية .
[He said: And doubt does not gain strength except regarding the existence of the sphere of fire, then regarding its dryness, and regarding the heat of air. And inference from effects is weak because other causes are possible, and (the argument) that if fire were hot and moist it would be air, and if air were cold and moist it would be water, is weaker because of the possibility of sharing in concomitants, especially those differing in intensity and weakness.] That is, the philosophers have differences concerning the number of elements. Some make the element one, the rest by transformation—some say fire, some say air, some say water, some say earth, some say vapor. Some make it two—some say fire and earth, some say water and earth, some say air and earth. Some make it three—some say fire, air, and earth, water being condensed air; some say air, water, and earth, fire being intensely hot air. They did not mention for these sayings any plausible proof that contradicts the correct conviction (al-istiqrār al-ṣaḥīḥ), so they would repel the opinion that the elements are four with the mentioned qualities. And doubt does not gain strength except in three matters: First, the existence of the sphere of fire—there is no way to prove it. The inference from meteors, claiming they are thick smoke that ignites upon reaching the sphere of fire, is weak because it is possible that it has another cause, especially on Islamic principles, and that what is seen of flames and fires is air that has become intensely hot, not a separate element. That is it. Second, the dryness of fire, meaning difficulty of accepting forms and leaving them. The way to such things is experiment and observation, but there is no place for them in the pure fire surrounding the air according to their claim. As for the mixed fire on the surface of the earth, it is obviously contrary to that. And the inference that the property of heat is to destroy moistures, and pure fire is extremely hot, so it follows that it is extremely dry, is weak, because its destruction of natural moisture (interpreted as ease of accepting and leaving forms) is not conceded. Rather, it only means wetness and watery parts, and what is like that; it does not necessitate that it is dry in itself, as in pure air. Third, the heat of air—there is no proof for it in pure air, apart from the reflection of rays. Do you not see that the higher it is from the earth, the colder it becomes until it becomes freezing? It is said that this is due to adjacency to the two cold things, that is, water and earth, with the removal of the impediment, that is, the reflection of rays, but this is not conceded. As for the inference that fire is hot, so if it were moist it would be air, and that air is moist, so if it were cold it would be water, it is extremely weak, because sharing in concomitants, especially concomitants differing in intensity and weakness specific to each of the things entailed, does not necessitate union of the things entailed in quiddity.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of three philosophical doubts concerning the elemental spheres. The author first quotes the matn stating that doubt only gains strength in three matters: the existence of the sphere of fire, its dryness, and the heat of air. The matn also notes that inference from effects is weak due to alternative explanations, and the argument from necessary properties is weaker because of possible shared concomitants. The commentary then explains that earlier philosophers disagree on the number of elements (one, two, three, or four) but these alternatives lack probative force. The commentary then addresses each doubt in turn. First doubt: the existence of a sphere of fire cannot be proven; the argument from meteors (shuhub) is weak because meteors could be due to other causes, and on Islamic principles what we see as fire may be intensely hot air, not a distinct element. Second doubt: the dryness of fire (meaning resistance to shaping) is not empirically verifiable for pure fire; earthly fire is not dry. The argument that extreme heat destroys moisture and thus implies extreme dryness is rejected because 'destruction of moisture' in this context does not mean the fire itself is dry; it only removes wetness without making fire dry. Third doubt: the heat of pure air is not proven; the observation that air gets colder at higher altitudes contradicts the claim. The counterargument that this is due to adjacency to cold bodies and removal of the heat-reflecting barrier is not conceded. The argument that if fire were moist it would be air, and if air were cold it would be water, is weak because shared concomitants (especially varying in intensity) do not imply identity of essence. Thus all three doubts are refuted.

Key Terms
يبوسة النار
التشكلات
الرطوبة الطبيعية
الهواء الصرف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Extreme heat necessarily implies extreme dryness.
Correction: Destruction of moisture does not entail that the heat source is dry; it may only remove wetness from other things.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the inference that pure fire is extremely dry because it destroys moisture?
A) Pure fire is not hot.
B) Destruction of moisture does not make the fire itself dry.
C) Earthly fire is also dry.
D) The philosophers never claimed that.

Key Takeaway
The dryness of pure fire is empirically unverifiable and logically not entailed by its heat; the inference fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever been absolutely certain of a belief about your deen based purely on logical reasoning, only to realize later you lacked direct evidence? What did that do to your conviction, and are you willing to re-examine that belief today?
?
The philosophers assumed fire must be dry because it burns away moisture. What assumptions do you hold about your own spiritual state—perhaps thinking you are righteous because of your outward actions—that might be similarly flawed? How can you seek direct evidence from the Quran and Sunnah instead?
?
This lesson challenges an inference that seemed obvious. Is there an area of your life where you have accepted an obvious-seeming conclusion about God's attributes or His creation without verifying it against revelation and sound reason? Will you commit to investigating it this week?

Refutation of the Doubt about the Heat of Air
The author refutes the philosophical doubt about the heat of air.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that pure air is hot is not supported by evidence and why the logical argument from shared properties fails.

Core Idea
Air's heat is not proven; observation shows it gets colder with altitude, and logical arguments are weak.

The third doubt is about the heat of air. Philosophers claim air is hot based on the reflection of rays and a logical argument that if air were cold it would be water. The author refutes both: observation shows air gets colder at higher altitudes, and the logical argument fails because shared properties do not imply identity of essence. This lesson examines the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on heat of air
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two arguments: the empirical observation and the logical argument.
2
Examine the empirical refutation
The author points out that higher air is colder, contrary to the claim. The counter-argument is not conceded.
3
Examine the logical refutation
The logical argument that shared concomitants imply identity is weak because concomitants can differ in intensity and not entail unity of quiddity.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
This demonstrates that inference from shared properties is insufficient to prove identity of essence.

Refuting the Claims about Fire
Refutes claims about fire: thick smoke and dryness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the weaknesses in the philosophical arguments that fire is thick smoke and that fire must be extremely dry due to its heat.

Core Idea
Observation of mixed fire does not prove properties of pure fire.

Philosophers claimed fire is thick smoke that ignites upon reaching the celestial sphere, and that its extreme heat makes it extremely dry. The author systematically shows these claims are based on flawed reasoning and unwarranted extrapolations from observable fire to pure fire. We will examine the criticisms and understand the proper methodological boundaries.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the two main points: the refutation of the 'thick smoke' claim and the refutation of the 'dryness from heat' inference.
This passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the two arguments being refuted
Find where the author states the philosophical claim and then presents his criticism. The first is about fire being thick smoke, the second about dryness.
3
Understand the key distinction
The author distinguishes between 'mixed fire' on earth and 'pure fire' alleged by philosophers. He argues that observation applies only to the former.
This is a crucial methodological point.
4
Analyze the dryness argument
Consider why the inference that extreme heat causes extreme dryness is weak. The author redefines 'moisture' and shows that destroying wetness does not make something dry in the relevant sense.
Compare with pure air, which is not dry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
زعماً منهم أنها دخان غليظ، يشتعل بالوصول إلى كرة النار ضعيف لجواز أن يكون لها سبب غير ذلك، سيما على القواعد الإسلامية، وأن يكون ما يشاهد من الشعل والنيران هواء اشتدت حرارته لا عنصراً برأسه. ذلك هو الثاني: يبوسة النار بمعنى عسر قبول التشكلات وتركها. فإن الطريق إلى أمثال التجربة والمشاهدة ولا مجال لهما في النار الصرفة المحيطة بالهواء على زعمهم، وأما المخلوطة التي على وجه الأرض فظاهر أنها بخلاف ذلك، والاستدلال بأن شأن الحرارة إفناء الرطوبات، والنار الصرفة في غاية الحرارة، فيلزم أن تكون في غاية اليبوسة ضعيف، لأن إفناءها للرطوبة الطبيعية المفسرة بسهولة قبول التشكيلات وتركها غير مسلم، بل إنما تعنى البلة والأجزاء المائية وما هو كذلك، لا يلزم أن يكون يابساً في نفسه كما في الهواء الصرف. الثالث: حرارة الهواء فإنه لا دليل على إثباتها في الهواء الصرف، عن انعكاس الأشعة، ألا ترى أنه كلما كان أرفع من الأرض كان أبرد إلى أن يصير زمهريراً. يقال إن ذلك بسبب مجاورة الباردين. أعني الماء والأرض مع زوال المانع . أعني انعكاس الأشعة، فغير مسلّم . وأما الاستدلال بأن النار حارة، فلو كانت رطبـة لكانت هواء، وبأن الهواء رطب، فلو كان بارداً لكان ماء ففي غاية الضعف لأن الاشتراك في اللوازم سيما اللوازم المختلفة بالشدة والضعف المختص بكل من الملزومات بعض تلك المختلفات لا يوجب اتحاد الملزومات في الماهية.
Claiming that it is thick smoke, igniting upon reaching the sphere of fire, is weak because it is possible that it has another cause, especially according to Islamic principles, and that what is seen of flames and fires is air whose heat has intensified, not an element by itself. That is the second [point]: The dryness of fire, meaning difficulty in accepting shapes and leaving them. For the way to such matters is experiment and observation, and they have no place in pure fire surrounding the air according to their claim; as for the mixed [fire] on the surface of the earth, it is clearly contrary to that. And the inference that the nature of heat is to destroy moistures, and pure fire is extreme in heat, therefore it must be extreme in dryness, is weak, because its destruction of natural moisture, interpreted as ease of accepting shapes and leaving them, is not conceded; rather, it concerns wetness and aqueous parts and the like; it does not necessitate that it be dry in itself, as in pure air. The third [point]: The heat of air. There is no proof for establishing it in pure air, due to the reflection of rays. Do you not see that the higher it is from the earth, the colder it becomes until it becomes freezing? It is said that this is due to adjacency to the two cold [bodies], namely water and earth, with the removal of the obstacle, namely the reflection of rays, but that is not conceded. As for the inference that fire is hot, for if it were moist it would be air, and that air is moist, for if it were cold it would be water, it is extremely weak, because sharing in concomitants, especially concomitants varying in intensity and weakness, each of the implicated things having some of those varying [concomitants], does not necessitate identity of the implicated things in essence.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage contains two refutations of philosophical claims about the properties of fire and air. The author first dismisses the claim that fire is thick smoke igniting at the celestial sphere as weak, since it could have another cause, especially under Islamic principles. The second point concerns the dryness of fire, defined as resistance to shaping. The author argues that experiment and observation, the proper methods for such matters, cannot be applied to pure fire surrounding the air as claimed by philosophers. For mixed fire on earth, observation shows the contrary. The argument that extreme heat destroys moisture and thus fire must be extremely dry is flawed because the 'moisture' destroyed is wetness, not the technical moisture defined as ease of shaping. Destroying wetness does not entail dryness—as seen in pure air, which is not dry despite having no wetness. The third point concerns the heat of air. No proof exists for heat in pure air; rather, higher air is colder. The explanation that this is due to adjacency to cold bodies (water and earth) after removing the obstacle of reflected rays is not conceded. The inference that fire is hot (so if moist it would be air) and air is moist (so if cold it would be water) is extremely weak, because shared concomitants, especially those varying in intensity, do not necessitate identity of essences. The author thus systematically undermines the philosophers' elemental attributions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That properties observed in mixed fire (on earth) apply to pure fire among the celestial spheres.
Correction: Observation only applies to mixed fire; pure fire is inaccessible to experiment, and philosophers' claims about it are speculative.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the inference that fire must be extremely dry because it destroys moisture considered weak?
A) Because fire is actually moist.
B) Because the 'moisture' destroyed is wetness, not the ease of shaping that defines dryness.
C) Because pure fire cannot be observed.
D) Because dryness is not caused by heat.

Key Takeaway
Philosophical claims about pure fire's properties are weak because they rely on unobservable assumptions and conflate different meanings of moisture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that a person's outward behavior (like the mixed fire) reveals their true inner state (like pure fire), only to later realize you were mistaken? What would it take for you to humbly admit that you cannot know the unseen (al-ghayb) without divine guidance, and to withhold judgment about others' hearts?
?
This lesson shows that even 'obvious' conclusions (like fire being dry) fall apart under scrutiny. What is one belief you hold about Allah's creation – for example, that rain is caused by clouds, or that food satisfies hunger – that, if you are honest, might be based on habit rather than on the Qur’anic teaching that Allah is the sole Creator of all effects? Will you today recite 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' as a conscious acknowledgment that you depend on Him for every outcome?
?
The philosophers relied on their own reasoning without submitting to Islamic principles. In what area of your life – such as your career, relationships, or health – are you relying on your own plan and analysis more than on tawakkul (trust in Allah)? Write down one practical step you will take this week to actively rely on Allah's decree rather than your own calculations.
